Using Sensible Premises as a Standard for Reasoning
Author explains the method of using sensible and necessary premises to derive certain conclusions and overcome estimative resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological method described in this passage for achieving certainty in abstract matters by using sensible and necessary premises as a standard.

Core Idea
Certainty in abstract matters is built on premises that even the estimative faculty cannot deny.

This passage presents a powerful epistemological strategy: to achieve certainty in abstract or obscure matters, reason must first establish a foundation of premises that are undeniable to both sense and estimation. Once a valid syllogistic form is constructed from these solid premises and accepted, the same logical form can be transferred to more abstract subjects. The resistance of the estimative faculty (wahm) to the new conclusion is then seen as a limitation of that faculty, not a flaw in the argument. This method provides a standard (miyar) for reasoning that ensures confidence in the truth of conclusions reached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem and the proposed solution
The author begins by referencing a need for a stratagem to escape misguidance from two deceptive judges (sense and estimation). The solution is to use premises that are universally agreed upon (sensible and necessary).
The 'two judges' likely refer to the external senses and the estimative faculty, which can be deceived.
2
Trace the reason's stratagem
Reason first gathers premises from sensible and necessary matters that both sense and estimation can affirm. It arranges these premises in an undisputed order (a valid syllogism) and derives a conclusion that estimation cannot deny.
The order is the syllogistic form; the conclusion is accepted because the premises are undeniable and the form is valid.
3
Understand the transfer to obscure matters
Reason transfers the same arrangement of premises (the logical form) to abstract matters. When estimation denies the new conclusion, one realizes that the denial stems from estimation's own deficiency, not from the conclusion's falsity.
The transfer is of the logical structure, not the specific content.
4
Formulate the book's purpose
The author states the goal: to take sensible and necessary matters as a standard for reasoning, so that when transferred to obscure matters, one does not doubt the truth of what follows.
This establishes the epistemological foundation for the entire book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجرى الدم وإذا لاحظت بعين العقل هذه الأسرار التي نبهتك عليها استيقنت شدة حاجتك إلى تدبير حيلة في الخلاص من ضلال هذين الحاكمين . فإن قلت : فما الحيلة في الاحتياط مع ما وصفتمونه من شدة الرباط بهذه المغويات؛ فتأمل لطف حيل العقل فيه فإنه استدرج الحس والوهم إلى أمور يساعدانه على دركها من المشاهدات الموافقة للموهوم والمعقول، وأخذ منها مقدمات يساعده الوهم عليها، ورتبها ترتيباً لا ينازع فيه، واستنتج منها بالضرورة نتيجة لم يسع الوهم التكذيب بها، إذ كانت مأخوذة من الأمور التي لا يتخلّف الوهم والعقل عن القضاء بها، وهي العلوم التي لم يختلف فيها الناس من الضروريات والحسّيات واستسلمها من الحسن والوهم وارتهنها منهما، فصدقا بأن النتيجة اللازمة منها صادقة حقيقية، ثم نقلها العقل بعينها على ترتيبها إلى ما ينازع الوهم فيه وأخرج منها نتائج . فلما كذب الوهم بها وامتنع عن قبولها هان على العقل مؤونته، فإن المقدمات التي وضعها كان الوهم يصدّق بها على الترتيب الذي رتبه لانتاج النتيجة، فكأن الوهم قد سلم لزوم النتيجة منها فتحقق الناظر أن إباء الوهم عن قبول النتيجة بعد التصديق بالمقدمات والتصديق بصحة الترتيب المنتج لقصور في طباعه وجبلّته عن درك هذه النتيجة لا لكون هذه النتيجة كاذبة لأن ترتيب المقدمات منقول من موضع ساعد الوهم على التصديق بها . فإذن غرضنا في هذا الكتاب أن نأخذ من المحسوسات والضروريات الجبلية معياراً للنظر، حتى إذا نقلناه إلى الغوامض لم نشك في صدق ما يلزم منها.
The course of blood. And if you observe with the eye of reason these secrets that I have alerted you to, you will be certain of your intense need to devise a stratagem for deliverance from the misguidance of these two judges. If you say: So what is the stratagem for caution given what you have described of the severity of attachment to these seductions? Then reflect on the subtlety of reason's stratagem in it, for it enticed the sense and the estimation to matters that they both help it to grasp from observable things that agree with the estimated and the intelligible. And it took from them premises that estimation helps it with, and arranged them in an arrangement not disputed, and deduced from them necessarily a conclusion that estimation could not deny, since they were taken from matters that estimation and reason do not fail to judge, which are the knowledges that people have not differed over among the necessary and the sensory. And it surrendered itself from sense and estimation and took them as pledges from both, so they both affirmed that the necessary conclusion from them is truthful and real. Then reason transferred those very [premises] in their arrangement to what estimation disputes and extracted from them conclusions. And when estimation falsified them and refused to accept them, reason's burden became light, for the premises that it had laid down estimation would affirm in the arrangement it had arranged for producing the conclusion, so it was as if estimation had conceded the entailment of the conclusion. Thus the observer realizes that estimation's refusal to accept the conclusion after affirming the premises and affirming the validity of the productive arrangement is due to a deficiency in its nature and constitution from grasping this conclusion, not because this conclusion is false, since the arrangement of the premises is transferred from a place where estimation helped in affirming them. So then our purpose in this book is to take from the sensible things and the innate necessary things a standard for reasoning, so that when we transfer it to the obscure matters we do not doubt the truth of what necessarily follows from them.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of a method to achieve certainty in abstract intellectual matters. The author begins by referring to a previous discussion about two deceptive 'judges' (probably sense and estimation). He then describes how reason can employ a clever stratagem: it first uses sensory and necessary premises that both the estimative faculty (wahm) and reason cannot deny. These premises are organized into a valid syllogism, producing a conclusion that the estimation itself must accept. Once this structure is established as sound, reason transfers the same logical form to more obscure matters. Even if estimation resists the new conclusion, the thinker knows that the resistance is due to estimation's own limitation, not the conclusion's falsity. The author then states the book's purpose: to establish such a standard (miyar) from sensations and innate necessities. The argument follows a clear sequence: problem (need for a stratagem), solution (use agreed premises), validation (estimation concedes the inference), and application (transfer to obscure matters).

Key Terms
الوهم
الحس
الضروريات

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the purpose of using sensible and necessary premises as a standard?
A) To avoid the need for reasoning in obscure matters.
B) To transfer a valid syllogistic form to abstract subjects without doubting the conclusion.
C) To prove that the estimative faculty is always correct.
D) To reject all metaphysical inquiries.

Key Takeaway
The method described in this passage ensures that conclusions about abstract matters are as certain as the sensible and necessary premises they are derived from, by first validating the logical form on undeniable grounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a simple syllogism using a sensible premise (e.g., 'This fire is hot' from experience) and a necessary premise (e.g., 'Heat causes burning') to derive a conclusion about an unobserved object (e.g., 'This unseen fire will cause burning'). Explain how this mirrors the method described in the passage.
?
Identify a theological argument from Islamic kalam (e.g., the argument from contingency) and break it down into premises that are either sensible (mushahada) or necessary (daruriyya). Show how the transfer to an abstract conclusion (e.g., existence of a necessary being) is justified by the method.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Syllogism as Second Composition
Author introduces syllogism as second composition and uses building analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the syllogism (qiyas) as the second composition of propositions and distinguish it from the first composition (proposition formation).

Core Idea
A syllogism is to propositions as a brick is to a building: the second composition builds upon the first.

The author opens the Book of Syllogism by reminding us that we have already studied the first composition – how simple meanings combine into propositions. Now we move to the second composition, the syllogism, which combines propositions to yield new knowledge. This lesson unpacks the definition of syllogism as the second composition, contrasting it with the first composition and using the concrete analogy of brick-making to make the abstract concept accessible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two compositions
Locate in the Arabic the phrases 'التركيب الأول' (first composition) and 'التركيب الثاني' (second composition). The first composes simple meanings into propositions; the second composes propositions into syllogisms.
2
Understand the progression from simple to complex
Notice that the second composition depends on the first: just as you cannot build a brick wall without first making bricks, you cannot form a syllogism without first forming propositions. This is a hierarchical structure of logical reasoning.
3
Recognize the necessity of the second composition
The author states that the syllogism is 'التركيب الواجب في المركبات' (the necessary composition in compounds). Reflect on why logical reasoning cannot stop at propositions; new knowledge requires combining them.
In Islamic sciences, this is why usul al-fiqh uses syllogistic reasoning to derive rulings.
4
Compare the brick-making analogy
The author compares the builder gathering earth and water to form bricks (first composition) then arranging bricks into a house (second composition). Map each element: simple materials → simple meanings; brick → proposition; house → syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القياس اعلم أنا إذا فرغنا من مقدمات القياس وهو بيان المعاني المفردة ووجوه دلالة الألفاظ عليها وكيفية تأليف المعاني بالتركيب الخبري المشتمل على الموضوع والمحمول المُسمّى قضيّة وأحكامها وأقسامها فجدير بنا أن نخوض في بيان القياس فإنه التركيب الثاني، لأنه نظر في تركيب القضايا ليصير قياساً كما كان الأول نظراً في تركيب المعاني ليصير قضيّة. وهذا هو التركيب الواجب في المركبات. فباني البيت ينبغي له أن يسعى أولاً للجمع بين المفردات، أعني الماء والتراب والتبن، فيجمعها على شكل مخصوص ليصير لبناً، ثم يجمع اللبنات فيركبها تركيباً ثانياً ؛ كذلك ينبغي أن يكون صنيع الناظر في كل مركب، وكما أن اللين لا يصير لبناً إلا بمادة وصورة المادة التراب وما فيه، والصورة هو التربيع الحاصل بحصره في قالبه كذلك القياس المركب له مادة وصورة، المادة المقدمات اليقينية الصادقة فلا بدّ من طلبها ومعرفة مداركها، والصورة هي تأليف المقدمات على نوع من الترتيب مخصوص ولا بد من معرفته؛ فانقسم النظر فيه إلى أربعة فنون : المادة والصورة والمغلطات في القياس، وفصول متفرقة اللواحق .
Book of Syllogism. Know that when we have finished with the preliminaries of the syllogism – which are the exposition of simple meanings, the ways in which words indicate them, and the manner of composing meanings into the predicative composition containing subject and predicate called a proposition, its rules and its divisions – then it is proper for us to delve into the exposition of the syllogism, for it is the second composition. Because it is a consideration of the composition of propositions so as to become a syllogism, just as the first was a consideration of the composition of meanings so as to become a proposition. And this is the necessary composition in compounds. The builder of a house should first strive to gather the simple materials, namely water, earth, and straw, and combine them in a specific form to become a brick; then he gathers the bricks and composes them a second time. Likewise should be the work of the investigator in every compound. And just as the brick does not become a brick except through matter and form – the matter being the earth and what is in it, and the form being the squareness obtained by confining it in its mold – similarly the compound syllogism has matter and form. The matter is the certain true premises, so one must seek them and know their sources; and the form is the arrangement of the premises in a specific order, so one must know it. Therefore the inquiry into it divides into four arts: matter, form, fallacies in the syllogism, and miscellaneous chapters of appendices.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the Book of Syllogism (Kitab al-Qiyas). The author first reminds us that we have completed the preliminaries: the study of simple meanings, indication of words, and the composition of propositions (qadaya). Now we proceed to the second composition, which is the syllogism – a composition of propositions. He draws an analogy with building: first, one gathers raw materials (water, earth, straw) and shapes them into bricks (first composition), then arranges the bricks into a building (second composition). Similarly, the logician first composes simple concepts into propositions, then composes propositions into syllogisms. The author emphasizes that every compound has matter and form. For a brick, the matter is earth and the form is its square shape from the mold. For a syllogism, the matter is the certain, true premises (muqaddimat yaqiniyya sadiqa), and the form is the specific arrangement of those premises. Therefore, the study of syllogism is divided into four arts: (1) matter (what premises are acceptable), (2) form (the valid figures and moods), (3) fallacies (mughtalatat) that occur in syllogisms, and (4) miscellaneous chapters (fusuul mutafarriqa) which cover other related topics. This introduction sets the framework for the entire book. The author uses the house-building analogy to make the abstract concept of composition concrete. The matter/form distinction is crucial: a syllogism is not just any combination of premises; it must have the right material (true premises) and the right structure (valid form). This lesson is foundational for understanding the structure of the ensuing chapters.

Key Terms
قياس
قضية
تركيب أول
تركيب ثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first composition in logic?
A) Forming propositions from simple meanings
B) Forming syllogisms from propositions
C) Gathering earth and water to make bricks
D) Arranging premises in a valid figure

Key Takeaway
The syllogism is the second composition in logic, built upon the first composition of propositions, just as a brick building is built upon individual bricks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the propositions 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Nabidh is an intoxicant'. Combine them into a syllogism and identify which part corresponds to the first composition (propositions) and which to the second composition (syllogism).
?
In your own words, explain how the brick-making analogy illustrates the relationship between propositions and syllogisms, using an Islamic example of your choice (e.g., zakat on crops).

The Syllogism
intermediate

Matter and Form of the Syllogism
Author explains matter and form of syllogism using brick analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the matter (maddah) and form (surah) of a syllogism and explain why both are necessary for a sound argument.

Core Idea
A syllogism is only as sound as its premises (matter) and their arrangement (form).

The author extends the brick-making analogy to show that every syllogism has two essential aspects: matter (the premises themselves, which must be certain and true) and form (the specific order in which they are arranged). This lesson explores this distinction and its importance for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matter and form of a brick
From the analogy: the matter of a brick is earth and what is in it (water, straw); the form is the square shape obtained from the mold. Understand that both are necessary for a brick to exist.
2
Translate to the syllogism
The matter of a syllogism is the premises themselves (muqaddimat), and these must be certain and true (yaqiniyya sadiqa). The form is the arrangement of those premises in a specific order (a valid figure and mood).
3
Understand why truth alone is insufficient
Even if premises are true, an invalid form can lead to a false or unsupported conclusion. Conversely, a valid form cannot make false premises true. Both must be sound. Reflect on why the author emphasizes this distinction.
4
See how the four arts correspond
The author divides the study of syllogism into four arts: matter (seeking true premises), form (learning valid arrangements), fallacies (errors in matter or form), and appendices. This shows the practical application of the matter/form distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القياس اعلم أنا إذا فرغنا من مقدمات القياس وهو بيان المعاني المفردة ووجوه دلالة الألفاظ عليها وكيفية تأليف المعاني بالتركيب الخبري المشتمل على الموضوع والمحمول المُسمّى قضيّة وأحكامها وأقسامها فجدير بنا أن نخوض في بيان القياس فإنه التركيب الثاني، لأنه نظر في تركيب القضايا ليصير قياساً كما كان الأول نظراً في تركيب المعاني ليصير قضيّة. وهذا هو التركيب الواجب في المركبات. فباني البيت ينبغي له أن يسعى أولاً للجمع بين المفردات، أعني الماء والتراب والتبن، فيجمعها على شكل مخصوص ليصير لبناً، ثم يجمع اللبنات فيركبها تركيباً ثانياً ؛ كذلك ينبغي أن يكون صنيع الناظر في كل مركب، وكما أن اللين لا يصير لبناً إلا بمادة وصورة المادة التراب وما فيه، والصورة هو التربيع الحاصل بحصره في قالبه كذلك القياس المركب له مادة وصورة، المادة المقدمات اليقينية الصادقة فلا بدّ من طلبها ومعرفة مداركها، والصورة هي تأليف المقدمات على نوع من الترتيب مخصوص ولا بد من معرفته؛ فانقسم النظر فيه إلى أربعة فنون : المادة والصورة والمغلطات في القياس، وفصول متفرقة اللواحق .
Book of Syllogism. Know that when we have finished with the preliminaries of the syllogism – which are the exposition of simple meanings, the ways in which words indicate them, and the manner of composing meanings into the predicative composition containing subject and predicate called a proposition, its rules and its divisions – then it is proper for us to delve into the exposition of the syllogism, for it is the second composition. Because it is a consideration of the composition of propositions so as to become a syllogism, just as the first was a consideration of the composition of meanings so as to become a proposition. And this is the necessary composition in compounds. The builder of a house should first strive to gather the simple materials, namely water, earth, and straw, and combine them in a specific form to become a brick; then he gathers the bricks and composes them a second time. Likewise should be the work of the investigator in every compound. And just as the brick does not become a brick except through matter and form – the matter being the earth and what is in it, and the form being the squareness obtained by confining it in its mold – similarly the compound syllogism has matter and form. The matter is the certain true premises, so one must seek them and know their sources; and the form is the arrangement of the premises in a specific order, so one must know it. Therefore the inquiry into it divides into four arts: matter, form, fallacies in the syllogism, and miscellaneous chapters of appendices.

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the Book of Syllogism (Kitab al-Qiyas). The author first reminds us that we have completed the preliminaries: the study of simple meanings, indication of words, and the composition of propositions (qadaya). Now we proceed to the second composition, which is the syllogism – a composition of propositions. He draws an analogy with building: first, one gathers raw materials (water, earth, straw) and shapes them into bricks (first composition), then arranges the bricks into a building (second composition). For a brick, the matter is earth and the form is its square shape from the mold. Therefore, the study of syllogism is divided into four arts: (1) matter (what premises are acceptable), (2) form (the valid figures and moods), (3) fallacies (mughtalatat) that occur in syllogisms, and (4) miscellaneous chapters (fusuul mutafarriqa) which cover other related topics. This introduction sets the framework for the entire book. The author uses the house-building analogy to make the abstract concept of composition concrete. The matter/form distinction is crucial: a syllogism is not just any combination of premises; it must have the right material (true premises) and the right structure (valid form). This lesson is foundational for understanding the structure of the ensuing chapters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every act of worship is commanded by Allah; the five daily prayers are acts of worship; therefore, the five daily prayers are commanded by Allah.' Identify the matter (the premises) and the form (the arrangement). Is the matter true? Is the form valid?
?
Construct a syllogism about an Islamic ruling (e.g., zakat on crops) with true matter but invalid form, then correct the form to make it valid.

Definition of Qiyas and Its Types
Definition and classification of qiyas within argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qiyas and identify its four types, distinguishing it from induction and analogy.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a composite statement yielding a necessary conclusion; its validity depends on form, not truth.

The author begins by placing qiyas within the broader category of arguments (hujja). He then provides a precise definition, emphasizing that qiyas is defined by the logical necessity of its conclusion when premises are granted, not by the actual truth of those premises. This distinction is crucial for understanding formal logic and its application in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of qiyas
Find the sentence that defines qiyas. It says: 'القياس : أنه قول مُؤلّف إذا سلم ما أورد فيه من القضايا لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر اضطراراً'. Identify the key components: 'composite statement', 'if premises granted', 'by itself', 'another statement necessarily follows'.
2
Extract the conditions for qiyas
From the definition, note two conditions: (1) the statement must be composite (mu'allaf), (2) the conclusion must follow necessarily (lazima) by itself (li-dhatihi) when premises are granted.
The necessity is internal to the form, not dependent on actual truth.
3
Categorize qiyas within hujja
The passage states that arguments (hujja) are of three types: qiyas, istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). Distinguish qiyas from the other two: qiyas is deductive, while istiqra' and tamthil are not necessarily deductive.
4
List the four types of qiyas
The author mentions four types: hamli (categorical), sharti muttasil (conditional conjunctive), sharti munfasil (conditional disjunctive), and qiyas khalf (reductio ad absurdum). Write them down with their Arabic names.
These four are all species under the genus qiyas.
5
Understand the premise/conclusion terminology
Note the author's terminological precision: before being placed in the argument, propositions are called 'qadaya'; after, they are 'muqaddimat' (premises). The conclusion is 'matlub' before inference and 'natija' after.
This highlights the shift in perspective from content to logical role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الأول في صورة القياس: والقياس أحد أنواع الحجج، والحُجّة هي التي يُؤتى بها في إثبات ما تمس الحاجة إلى إثباته من العلوم التصديقية، وهي ثلاثة أقسام: قياس واستقراء وتمثيل . والقياس أربعة أنواع حملي وشرطي متصل وشرطي منفصل وقياس خلف، ولنسم الجميع أصناف الحجّة . وحدّ القياس : أنه قول مُؤلّف إذا سلم ما أورد فيه من القضايا لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر اضطراراً وإذا أوردت القضايا في الحُجّة سُمّيت عند ذلك مقدمات . وتُسمّى قضايا قبل الوضع، كما أن القول اللازم عنه يُسمّى قبل اللزوم مطلوباً وبعد اللزوم نتيجة. وليس من شرط في أن يُسمّى قياساً أن يكون مسلّم القضايا بل من شرطه أن يكون بحيث إذا سلمت قضاياه لزم منها النتيجة، وربما تكون القضايا غير واجبة التسليم ونحن نُسمّيه قياساً لكونه بحيث لو سلم للزمت النتيجة. فلنبدأ بالحملي من أنواع القياس والحجج .
The first consideration: on the form of qiyas. Qiyas is one of the types of arguments. The argument (hujja) is that which is brought to prove something that is needed to be proven from among the assertive sciences. It has three divisions: qiyas, induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). Qiyas has four types: categorical (hamli), conditional conjunctive (sharti muttasil), conditional disjunctive (sharti munfasil), and reductio ad absurdum (qiyas khalf). Let us call them all the species of argument. The definition of qiyas: it is a composite statement such that when the propositions included in it are granted, then by itself another statement necessarily follows. And when the propositions are used in an argument, they are at that point called premises (muqaddimat). They are called propositions before being used, just as the statement that necessarily follows is called the sought (matlub) before the implication and the conclusion (natija) after the implication. It is not a condition for something to be called qiyas that its propositions be actually granted; rather, it is a condition that it be such that if its propositions were granted, the conclusion would follow from them. Sometimes the propositions are not necessarily to be granted, yet we still call it qiyas because it is such that if they were granted, the conclusion would follow. So let us begin with the categorical type of qiyas among the types of argument.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section. The author begins by stating that the first consideration in the study of qiyas is its form. He then defines qiyas as one kind of argument. He distinguishes three main types of arguments used to prove assertive sciences: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). Then he lists four types of qiyas: categorical (hamli), conditional conjunctive (sharti muttasil), conditional disjunctive (sharti munfasil), and qiyas khalf (reductio ad absurdum). He indicates that these four are all species of the broader category 'hujja'. Next, he gives a precise definition of qiyas: it is a composite statement (qawl mu'allaf) such that if the propositions in it are granted, then another statement necessarily follows from it by itself. This definition emphasizes two key conditions: (1) the premises must be granted, and (2) the conclusion must follow by itself without external aid. The author then clarifies the terms: the propositions used in the argument are called 'muqaddimat' (premises) only after being placed in the argument; before that they are just 'qadaya' (propositions). Similarly, the resulting statement is called 'matlub' (sought) before the inference and 'natija' (conclusion) after the inference. He also notes that it is not a condition for a qiyas that its premises are actually true or accepted; it suffices that if they were accepted, the conclusion would follow. This is important because logicians study qiyas even when premises are false, as long as the form is valid. Finally, he announces that he will begin with the categorical syllogism.

القياس
المقدمات

Qiyas is defined by its form: a composite statement from which a conclusion necessarily follows when premises are granted, regardless of their actual truth.

The Categorical Syllogism: Composition and Example
Author explains the categorical syllogism, its composition from two premises, and why it has only three terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises and three terms in a categorical syllogism from the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
A categorical syllogism is two premises joined by a shared middle term.

The author introduces the first species of syllogism, the categorical (hamli), also called conjunctive (iqtirani). He gives a concrete example from kalam: the proof that every body is originated. We will dissect this example to see the two premises and the three terms that make it valid.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find how the author defines al-qiyas al-hamli. He says it is composed of two premises. Underline the terms 'مقدمتين' (two premises).
This distinguishes it from conditional syllogisms.
2
Identify the example's premises
In the example 'كل جسم مؤلف وكل مؤلف محدث', identify the first premise: 'كل جسم مؤلف' (Every body is composed). The second premise: 'كل مؤلف محدث' (Everything composed is originated).
These are both categorical propositions.
3
Count the terms per premise
Each premise has a subject and a predicate. First premise: subject = جسم (body), predicate = مؤلف (composed). Second premise: subject = مؤلف (composed), predicate = محدث (originated). Write down the four term occurrences.
4
Spot the repeated term
Notice that 'مؤلف' (composed) appears in both premises. It is the shared term. Because of this repetition, the total distinct terms are three: body, composed, originated.
This is the middle term that connects the premises.
5
Confirm the conclusion
The conclusion 'كل جسم محدث' (every body is originated) follows because the middle term links the minor term (body) to the major term (originated). Write down the three terms with their roles: minor term (body), middle term (composed), major term (originated).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الأول : القياس الحملي القسمة الأولى له باعتبار أجزائه : قد يُسمّى قياساً اقترانياً، وقد يُسمّى جزمياً. وهو مركب من مقدمتين مثل قولنا كلّ جسم مؤلف وكل مؤلف محدث فيلزم منه أن كلّ جسم محدث ; فهذا القياس مركب من مقدمتين، وكلّ مقدمة تشتمل على موضوع ومحمول فيكون مجموع الآحاد التي تنحل إليه هذه المقدمات أربعة، إلا أن واحداً منها يتكرّر فالمجموع إذن ثلاثة وهو أقل ما ينحل إليه قياس، إذ أقل ما يلتئم منه القياس مقدمتان، وأقل ما ينتظم منه المقدمة معنيان: أحدهما موضوع والآخر محمول. ولا بد أن يكون واحد مكرّراً مشتركاً في المقدمتين، فإنه
The first species: the categorical syllogism. Its first division in terms of its parts: it is sometimes called conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani) and sometimes called assertive (jazmi). It is composed of two premises, such as our statement: 'Every body is composed, and everything composed is originated; therefore it follows that every body is originated.' This syllogism is composed of two premises, and each premise consists of a subject and a predicate. Therefore the total number of individuals that these premises resolve into is four, except that one of them is repeated. So the total is three, which is the minimum number into which a syllogism can be resolved. Because the minimum from which a syllogism is formed is two premises, and the minimum from which a premise is composed is two meanings: one subject and one predicate. And it is necessary that one be repeated and shared in the two premises, because...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage introduces the categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli), also called conjunctive (iqtirani) or assertive (jazmi). The author states that it is composed of two premises. He gives a classic example from kalam: 'Every body is composed (murakkab), and everything composed is originated (muhdath), therefore every body is originated.' This is a valid syllogism. The author then explains the composition arithmetically: each premise has two terms (subject and predicate), so the two premises together have four terms. However, one term is repeated (in this case, 'composed' appears in both premises as the predicate of the first and subject of the second). Thus the total distinct terms are three. This is the fundamental structure of a categorical syllogism: it must have exactly three terms (the middle term, major term, and minor term). The author states that the minimum number of premises is two, and each premise must have at least two components (subject and predicate). It is necessary that one term be shared (the middle term) between the two premises to connect them. This shared term is what allows the deduction of the conclusion. The passage thus establishes the basic anatomy of the categorical syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس الحملي
المقدمة
الموضوع
المحمول

B) 3

D) 1

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism always has exactly three terms: the middle term connects the two premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Why Three Terms? The Minimum Components of a Syllogism
Author explains the categorical syllogism, its composition from two premises, and why it has only three terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a categorical syllogism cannot have fewer than two premises or fewer than three terms.

Core Idea
Two premises and three terms are the irreducible core of a syllogism.

The author delves into the minimum requirements for any syllogism: two premises and three terms. He shows that without these, a deduction cannot occur. This lesson unpacks the arithmetic and the logical necessity behind these minima.

Guided Path
1
Find the claim about minimum premises
The author states 'أقل ما يلتئم منه القياس مقدمتان' (the minimum from which a syllogism is formed is two premises). Underline 'مقدمتان' and note: a single premise cannot produce a new conclusion directly.
This is because a syllogism needs a middle term to link two distinct ideas.
2
Find the claim about minimum terms
He says 'أقل ما ينتظم منه المقدمة معنيان: أحدهما موضوع والآخر محمول' (the minimum from which a premise is composed is two meanings: one subject and one predicate). So each premise has two term-slots.
3
Understand the subtraction reasoning
He calculates: two premises × two terms each = four term occurrences. But one term must be repeated (shared) for the premises to be connected. So distinct terms = 4 - 1 = 3. Write this equation in your notes.
The shared term is the middle term; without it, no conclusion follows.
4
Recognize the necessity of a shared term
The author explicitly says 'ولا بد أن يكون واحد مكرّراً مشتركاً في المقدمتين' (it is necessary that one be repeated and shared in the two premises). This sharing is what allows the conclusion to connect the other two terms.
In the example, 'مؤلف' (composed) is the shared term.

The Hierarchy of Arguments: Hujja, Qiyas, Istiqra, Tamthil
Author defines hujja and lists its three subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three types of arguments (qiyas, istiqra, tamthil) and explain how qiyas fits into the broader category of hujja.

Core Idea
The syllogism is one type of argument, alongside induction and analogy.

In this lesson, we examine how the author situates qiyas within a broader framework of arguments (hujaj) used to establish assertoric knowledge. Understanding this classification is essential for appreciating the role of syllogistic reasoning in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the broader category
In the Arabic, find the sentence: 'والقياس أحد أنواع الحجج'. This tells you that qiyas is a type of hujja. Then locate the definition of hujja: 'الحُجّة هي التي يُؤتى بها في إثبات ما تمس الحاجة إلى إثباته من العلوم التصديقية'. So hujja is what is used to establish something needed in the assertoric sciences.
2
List the three subdivisions
The author says: 'وهي ثلاثة أقسام: قياس واستقراء وتمثيل'. Write down these three: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), tamthil (analogy). Note that qiyas is one of them.
Induction and analogy are also valid arguments but may not yield certainty.
3
Understand the relationship
The author then says qiyas has four types (which will be covered later). So hujja is the genus, containing qiyas, istiqra, tamthil as species. And qiyas itself is a genus for four types.
This hierarchical classification is common in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الأول : في صورة القياس
والقياس أحد أنواع الحجج، والحُجّة هي التي يُؤتى بها في إثبات ما تمس الحاجة إلى إثباته من العلوم التصديقية، وهي ثلاثة أقسام: قياس واستقراء وتمثيل . والقياس أربعة أنواع حملي وشرطي متصل وشرطي منفصل وقياس خلف، ولنسم الجميع أصناف الحجّة . وحدّ القياس : أنه قول مُؤلّف إذا سلم ما أورد فيه من القضايا لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر اضطراراً وإذا أوردت القضايا في الحُجّة سُمّيت عند ذلك مقدمات . وتُسمّى قضايا قبل الوضع، كما أن القول اللازم عنه يُسمّى قبل اللزوم مطلوباً وبعد اللزوم نتيجة. وليس من شرط في أن يُسمّى قياساً أن يكون مسلّم القضايا بل من شرطه أن يكون بحيث إذا سلمت قضاياه لزم منها النتيجة، وربما تكون القضايا غير واجبة التسليم ونحن نُسمّيه قياساً لكونه بحيث لو سلم للزمت النتيجة. فلنبدأ بالحملي من أنواع القياس والحجج .
The First Investigation: On the Form of the Syllogism
And the syllogism is one of the types of arguments. The argument is that which is brought in establishing what there is a need to establish from among the assertoric sciences. It has three divisions: syllogism, induction, and analogy. The syllogism has four types: categorical, conditional conjunctive, conditional disjunctive, and the syllogism of reductio. Let us name all of them the varieties of the argument. The definition of the syllogism: it is a composed statement such that if the propositions contained in it are conceded, there follows from it, by virtue of itself, another statement necessarily. When the propositions are adduced in the argument, they are at that point called premises. They are called propositions before being posited, just as the statement that follows from it is called the sought before the entailment and the conclusion after the entailment. It is not a condition for being called a syllogism that its propositions be actually conceded; rather, its condition is that it be such that if its propositions are conceded, the conclusion follows from them. Perhaps the propositions are not necessarily conceded, yet we call it a syllogism because it is such that if they were conceded, the conclusion would follow. So let us begin with the categorical among the types of syllogisms and arguments.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage opens a new investigation into the form of the syllogism. The author first situates the syllogism within the broader category of 'argument' (hujja), which is used to establish assertoric knowledge (al-‘ulum al-tasdiqiyya). Arguments are of three types: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra’), and analogy (tamthil). The syllogism itself has four types: categorical (hamli), conditional conjunctive (sharti muttasil), conditional disjunctive (sharti munfasil), and the syllogism of reductio (qiyas khalf). The author then defines the syllogism technically: it is a composed statement (qawl mu’allaf) such that if the propositions (qadaya) included in it are conceded (sullima), there necessarily follows from it, by virtue of its own form, another statement. This is the core definition of qiyas in the Aristotelian tradition. The author then clarifies the terminology: the propositions when used in an argument are called premises (muqaddimat); before being so used they are called propositions simply. The conclusion (natija) is called the 'sought' (matlub) before the entailment and the 'conclusion' after. An important clarification follows: for a syllogism to be valid, it is not required that the premises be actually accepted as true; rather, the condition is that if they were accepted, the conclusion would necessarily follow. This distinguishes logical validity from actual truth. The author ends by announcing the plan to begin with the categorical syllogism.

The Definition of Qiyas and Its Essential Condition
Author defines qiyas and explains the condition of logical validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the technical definition of qiyas and explain why it does not require actually true premises.

Core Idea
A syllogism is valid if its conclusion follows necessarily from its premises, regardless of actual truth.

This lesson unpacks the precise definition of qiyas as given by the author. He emphasizes that the defining property of a syllogism is not the truth of its premises but the necessity of the entailment. This is a fundamental insight that separates logical validity from soundness.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence beginning with 'وحدّ القياس : أنه قول مُؤلّف...'. This is the definition of qiyas.
2
Break down the definition
The definition has several parts: it is a composed statement (قول مؤلف); if the propositions contained in it are conceded (إذا سلم ما أورد فيه من القضايا); there follows from it, by virtue of itself, another statement necessarily (لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر اضطراراً). So the essence is the necessary entailment.
The phrase 'لذاته' means the entailment is due to the form of the statement, not external factors.
3
Understand the condition of hypothetical concession
The author clarifies: 'وليس من شرط في أن يُسمّى قياساً أن يكون مسلّم القضايا بل من شرطه أن يكون بحيث إذا سلمت قضاياه لزم منها النتيجة.' This means actual concession is not required; only that if they were conceded, the conclusion would follow. This is the concept of logical validity.
This distinguishes a syllogism from a proof that is actually accepted.
4
Learn the terminology for premises and conclusion
The author explains: when propositions are used in an argument, they are called premises (مقدمات); before that, they are just propositions. The conclusion is called the sought (مطلوب) before entailment and result (نتيجة) after entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Let us name all of them the varieties of the argument. The definition of the syllogism: it is a composed statement such that if the propositions contained in it are conceded, there follows from it, by virtue of itself, another statement necessarily. When the propositions are adduced in the argument, they are at that point called premises. They are called propositions before being posited, just as the statement that follows from it is called the sought before the entailment and the conclusion after the entailment. It is not a condition for being called a syllogism that its propositions be actually conceded; rather, its condition is that it be such that if its propositions are conceded, the conclusion follows from them. Perhaps the propositions are not necessarily conceded, yet we call it a syllogism because it is such that if they were conceded, the conclusion would follow. Commentary
This passage opens a new investigation into the form of the syllogism. The author first situates the syllogism within the broader category of 'argument' (hujja), which is used to establish assertoric knowledge (al-‘ulum al-tasdiqiyya). Arguments are of three types: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra’), and analogy (tamthil). The syllogism itself has four types: categorical (hamli), conditional conjunctive (sharti muttasil), conditional disjunctive (sharti munfasil), and the syllogism of reductio (qiyas khalf). The author then defines the syllogism technically: it is a composed statement (qawl mu’allaf) such that if the propositions (qadaya) included in it are conceded (sullima), there necessarily follows from it, by virtue of its own form, another statement. This is the core definition of qiyas in the Aristotelian tradition. The author then clarifies the terminology: the propositions when used in an argument are called premises (muqaddimat); before being so used they are called propositions simply. The conclusion (natija) is called the 'sought' (matlub) before the entailment and the 'conclusion' after. An important clarification follows: for a syllogism to be valid, it is not required that the premises be actually accepted as true; rather, the condition is that if they were accepted, the conclusion would necessarily follow. This distinguishes logical validity from actual truth. The author ends by announcing the plan to begin with the categorical syllogism.

مقدمة
نتيجة
مطلوب

The essence of a syllogism is necessary entailment, not actual truth of premises.

The Four Types of Syllogism
Author lists the four types of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four types of qiyas and give a brief description of each.

Core Idea
Syllogisms come in four forms: categorical, conditional conjunctive, conditional disjunctive, and reductio.

After defining qiyas, the author enumerates its four main types. These are the fundamental forms of syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic. This lesson provides an overview so you can recognize them in later studies.

Guided Path
1
Find the list in the Arabic
Locate the sentence: 'والقياس أربعة أنواع حملي وشرطي متصل وشرطي منفصل وقياس خلف'. This is the list of four types.
2
Identify each type
Translate each: حملي (categorical), شرطي متصل (conditional conjunctive, i.e., If...then), شرطي منفصل (conditional disjunctive, i.e., Either...or), قياس خلف (reductio ad absurdum).
3
Understand the author's plan
The author says: 'فلنبدأ بالحملي من أنواع القياس والحجج' — he will start with the categorical. This indicates that the categorical is the foundational type.
In Islamic logic, the categorical syllogism is studied first and most extensively.

النظر الأول : في صورة القياس
والقياس أحد أنواع الحجج، والحُجّة هي التي يُؤتى بها في إثبات ما تمس الحاجة إلى إثباته من العلوم التصديقية، وهي ثلاثة أقسام: قياس واستقراء وتمثيل . والقياس أربعة أنواع حملي وشرطي متصل وشرطي منفصل وقياس خلف، ولنسم الجميع أصناف الحجّة . وحدّ القياس : أنه قول مُؤلّف إذا سلم ما أورد فيه من القضايا لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر اضطراراً وإذا أوردت القضايا في الحُجّة سُمّيت عند ذلك مقدمات . وتُسمّى قضايا قبل الوضع، كما أن القول اللازم عنه يُسمّى قبل اللزوم مطلوباً وبعد اللزوم نتيجة. وليس من شرط في أن يُسمّى قياساً أن يكون مسلّم القضايا بل من شرطه أن يكون بحيث إذا سلمت قضاياه لزم منها النتيجة، وربما تكون القضايا غير واجبة التسليم ونحن نُسمّيه قياساً لكونه بحيث لو سلم للزمت النتيجة. فلنبدأ بالحملي من أنواع القياس والحجج .
The First Investigation: On the Form of the Syllogism
And the syllogism is one of the types of arguments. The argument is that which is brought in establishing what there is a need to establish from among the assertoric sciences. It has three divisions: syllogism, induction, and analogy. The syllogism has four types: categorical, conditional conjunctive, conditional disjunctive, and the syllogism of reductio. Let us name all of them the varieties of the argument. The definition of the syllogism: it is a composed statement such that if the propositions contained in it are conceded, there follows from it, by virtue of itself, another statement necessarily. When the propositions are adduced in the argument, they are at that point called premises. They are called propositions before being posited, just as the statement that follows from it is called the sought before the entailment and the conclusion after the entailment. It is not a condition for being called a syllogism that its propositions be actually conceded; rather, its condition is that it be such that if its propositions are conceded, the conclusion follows from them. Perhaps the propositions are not necessarily conceded, yet we call it a syllogism because it is such that if they were conceded, the conclusion would follow. So let us begin with the categorical among the types of syllogisms and arguments.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Terms and Two Premises of the Categorical Syllogism
Author explains the three terms of a categorical syllogism and the naming of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle, major, and minor terms in a categorical syllogism and label the major and minor premises.

Core Idea
Every categorical syllogism has exactly three terms; the middle term connects the other two.

In this passage, the author breaks down the categorical syllogism into its basic components. You will learn to recognise the three terms that form the backbone of any valid syllogism and understand why the middle term is essential for drawing conclusions. This foundational skill is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic disciplines like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Look at the example: 'Every body is composed; everything composed is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' The first two statements are premises, the last is the conclusion.
The conclusion is often signalled by 'therefore' or its equivalent.
2
Extract the four terms
Each premise has a subject and predicate: premise1 has 'body' and 'composed'; premise2 has 'composed' and 'temporally originated'. That gives four occurrences but only three distinct concepts.
Write them down to see repetition.
3
Find the repeated term (middle)
The term 'composed' appears in both premises. This is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). It connects the other two terms.
Without a repeated term, no conclusion follows.
4
Identify minor and major terms
The subject of the conclusion is 'body' – that is the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar). The predicate of the conclusion is 'temporally originated' – that is the major term (al-hadd al-akbar).
The major term may be more general but can be equal.
5
Label the premises
The premise containing the major term (temporally originated) is the major premise (muqaddima kubra). The premise containing the minor term (body) is the minor premise (muqaddima sughra).
The middle term is not used to name the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الأول : القياس الحملي
القسمة الأولى له باعتبار أجزائه : قد يُسمّى قياساً اقترانياً، وقد يُسمّى جزمياً. وهو مركب من مقدمتين مثل قولنا كلّ جسم مؤلف وكل مؤلف محدث فيلزم منه أن كلّ جسم محدث ؛ فهذا القياس مركب من مقدمتين، وكلّ مقدمة تشتمل على موضوع ومحمول فيكون مجموع الآحاد التي تنحل إليه هذه المقدمات أربعة، إلا أن واحداً منها يتكرّر فالمجموع إذن ثلاثة وهو أقل ما ينحل إليه قياس، إذ أقل ما يلتئم منه القياس مقدمتان، وأقل ما ينتظم منه المقدمة معنيان: أحدهما موضوع والآخر محمول. ولا بد أن يكون واحد مكرّراً مشتركاً في المقدمتين، فإنه إن لم يكن كذلك تباينت المقدمتان ولم يتداخلا ولم تلزم من ازدواجهما النتيجة . فإذا قلت : كلّ جسم مؤلف ولم تتكلم في المقدمة الثانية عن الجسم ولا عن المؤلف، بل قلت مثلاً كلّ إنسان حيوان لم تلزم نتيجة من المقدمتين. فإذا عرفت انقسام كل قياس إلى ثلاثة أمور مفردة فاعلم أن هذه المفردات تُسمّى حدوداً، ولكلّ واحد من الحدود الثلاثة اسم مفرد ليتميز عن غيره. أما الحدّ المشترك فَيُسمّى الحدّ الأوسط، وأما الآخران فَيُسمّى أحدهما الحدّ الأكبر والآخر الأصغر. والأصغر هو الذي يكون موضوعاً في النتيجة، والأكبر هو الذي يكون محمولاً فيها . وإنما سُمّي أكبر لأنه يمكن أن يكون أعم من الموضوع وإن أمكن أن يكون مساوياً. وأما الموضوع فلا يتصوّر أن يكون أعم من المحمول، وإذا وضع كذلك كان الحكم كاذباً كقولك : كلّ حيوان إنسان، فإنه كاذب وعكسه صادق . ثم لما مست الحاجة إلى تعريف المقدمتين باسمين ولم يمكن أن يشتق اسمهما من الحدّ الأوسط لأنه مشترك فيهما اشتق اسمهما من الحدين الآخرين فَسُمِّي الذي فيه الحد الأكبر، وهو محمول النتيجة مقدمة كبرى والذي فيه موضوعها، وهو الأصغر، مقدمة صغرى ؛ فالقياس الذي أوردناه مثالاً فيه ثلاثة حدود الجسم والمؤلف والمحدث والمؤلف هو الحدّ الأوسط، والجسم هو الأصغر، والمحدث هو الحد الأكبر. وقولنا: كلّ جسم مؤلف هي المقدمة الصغرى، وقولنا : كلّ مؤلف محدث هي المقدمة الكبرى، واللازم عنه هو التقاء الحدّين الواقعين على الطرفين وهو المطلوب أولاً والنتيجة آخراً، وهو قولنا : فكلّ جسم محدث ؛ ومثاله من الفقه : كلّ مُسكر خمر وكلّ خمر حرام، فكل مسكر حرام فالمسكر والخمر والحرام حدود القياس، والخمر هو الحد الأوسط، والمسكر هو الحدّ الأصغر، والحرام هو الحد الأكبر، وقولنا : كل مسكر خمر هي الصغرى، وقولنا : كلّ خمر حرام هي المقدمة الكبرى، فهذه قسمة للقياس باعتبار أجزائه المفردة .
The First Type: The Categorical Syllogism
Its first division with respect to its parts: It may be called a 'conjunctive syllogism' or a 'categorical syllogism'. It is composed of two premises, such as our saying: 'Every body is composed' and 'Everything composed is temporally originated', so it follows that 'Every body is temporally originated'. So this syllogism is composed of two premises, and each premise contains a subject and a predicate. Thus the total number of individuals into which these premises resolve is four, except that one of them is repeated, so the total is three, and that is the minimum into which a syllogism resolves. For the minimum from which a syllogism is composed is two premises, and the minimum from which a premise is arranged is two meanings: one is the subject and the other is the predicate. And it is necessary that there be one that is repeated and common in the two premises; if it is not so, the two premises would be disparate, they would not interrelate, and from their coupling no conclusion would follow. So if you say: 'Every body is composed' and you do not mention in the second premise either body or composed, but rather you say, for example, 'Every human is an animal', no conclusion follows from the two premises. When you know that every syllogism divides into three single matters, know that these single matters are called 'terms' (hudud). Each of the three terms has a distinct name to distinguish it from others. As for the common term, it is called the 'middle term'. As for the other two, one of them is called the 'major term' and the other the 'minor term'. The minor term is that which is the subject in the conclusion, and the major term is that which is the predicate in it. It is only called 'major' because it can be more general than the subject, although it may be equal. As for the subject, it cannot be conceived to be more general than the predicate; if it is placed as such, the judgment is false, like your saying: 'Every animal is a human'—it is false, while its converse is true. Then when there was need to name the two premises, and it was not possible to derive their names from the middle term because it is common to both, their names were derived from the other two terms. So that premise which contains the major term—which is the predicate of the conclusion—is called the 'major premise', and that which contains its subject—which is the minor term—is called the 'minor premise'. So the syllogism we gave as an example has three terms: body, composed, and temporally originated. 'Composed' is the middle term, 'body' is the minor term, and 'temporally originated' is the major term. Our saying 'Every body is composed' is the minor premise, and our saying 'Every composed thing is temporally originated' is the major premise. The necessary consequent is the meeting of the two extreme terms, which is the sought at first and the conclusion at last, namely our saying: 'Therefore every body is temporally originated'. And an example from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden', so 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. So 'intoxicant', 'khamr', and 'forbidden' are the terms of the syllogism; 'khamr' is the middle term, 'intoxicant' is the minor term, and 'forbidden' is the major term. Our saying 'Every intoxicant is khamr' is the minor premise, and our saying 'Every khamr is forbidden' is the major premise. So this is a division of the syllogism with respect to its individual parts.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the first division of the categorical syllogism: its division according to its component parts. The author begins by noting that the categorical syllogism is also called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani) or 'categorical' (jazmi). It is composed of two premises. An example is given: 'Every body is composed; everything composed is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' The author then analyses the number of terms: each premise has a subject and a predicate, so four terms in total, but one term is repeated (the middle term), leaving three distinct terms. This is the minimum number of terms in a syllogism. If there is no term common to both premises, they remain unconnected and no conclusion follows. The author illustrates with a counterexample: 'Every body is composed; every human is an animal'—no conclusion. The three terms are called 'limits' (hudud): the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat), the major term (al-hadd al-akbar), and the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar). The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, the major term is the predicate of the conclusion. The major term is so called because it may be more general than the minor, though it may also be equal. The subject (minor) cannot be more general than the predicate (major) in a true universal affirmative proposition; if it is, the proposition is false (e.g., 'Every animal is human'). Since we need names for the two premises and cannot derive them from the middle term (as it is common), we name them based on the other two terms: the premise containing the major term is the major premise, and the one containing the minor term is the minor premise. The author then applies this to the first example, identifying the terms and premises. Finally, a fiqh example is given: 'Every intoxicant is khamr; every khamr is forbidden; therefore every intoxicant is forbidden.' This demonstrates the same structure using Islamic legal concepts. The lesson ends by stating that this is the division of the syllogism with respect to its individual parts.

حد أوسط
حد أكبر
حد أصغر
مقدمة كبرى
مقدمة صغرى
قياس اقتراني

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism must have exactly three terms, with the middle term appearing in both premises to link the minor and major terms, enabling a necessary conclusion.

The Three Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Author classifies the categorical syllogism into three figures based on the position of the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism by analyzing the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism depends solely on the middle term's position.

In this lesson, we explore how logicians classify categorical syllogisms into figures based on where the middle term appears in the premises. This classification is the foundation for determining a syllogism's validity. The passage from the coursebook defines three figures, each with a distinct placement of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. For example, in 'Every A is B; every B is C; therefore every A is C', the middle term is B.
In Islamic logic examples, the middle term is often a key concept linking the subject and predicate.
2
Check the position in each premise
Determine whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate in each premise. Write down its role in the first premise and in the second premise.
The order of premises does not matter; only the position within each premise counts.
3
Apply the figure classification rules
Use these rules: If the middle term is predicate in one premise and subject in the other, it is the first figure. If it is predicate in both premises, it is the second figure. If it is subject in both premises, it is the third figure.
This passage presents three figures; later logicians add a fourth.
4
Practice with a Kalam example
Consider the proof from temporality: 'Everything that changes is temporal; the world changes; therefore the world is temporal.' Identify the middle term ('changes') and note its positions: predicate in the major premise ('everything that changes is temporal' – predicate is 'temporal', so 'changes' is subject? Wait, careful. Actually: major premise: 'Everything that changes is temporal' – subject: 'everything that changes', predicate: 'temporal'. So 'changes' is part of the subject. Minor premise: 'The world changes' – subject: 'the world', predicate: 'changes'. So the middle term 'changes' is predicate in the minor and subject in the major? No, in the major, 'changes' is inside the subject. That's tricky. Better example: 'Every composed thing is temporally originated; the body is composed; therefore the body is temporally originated.' Here middle term 'composed' is predicate in the minor and subject in the major? Actually: 'Every composed thing is temporally originated' – subject: 'every composed thing', predicate: 'temporally originated'. So 'composed' is part of the subject. In 'the body is composed', 'composed' is predicate. So it's first figure: middle term is predicate in one (minor) and subject in the other (major). So that works.
Ensure you correctly parse the propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الثانية لهذا القياس (الحملي) باعتبار كيفية وضع الحد الأوسط عند الطرفين الآخرين : وهذه الكيفية تُسمّى شكلاً، والحد الأوسط إما أن يكون محمولاً في إحدى المقدمتين موضوعاً في الأخرى كما أوردناه من المثال، فَيُسمّى شكلاً أولاً، وإما أن يكون محمولاً في المقدمتين جميعاً ويُسمّى الشكل الثاني، وإما أن يكون موضوعاً فيهما ويُسمّى الشكل الثالث
The second division of this (categorical) syllogism is with respect to the manner of placing the middle term in relation to the other two terms: this manner is called 'figure'. The middle term either is a predicate in one premise and a subject in the other, as in the example we gave—it is called the first figure; or it is a predicate in both premises—it is called the second figure; or it is a subject in both—it is called the third figure.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage introduces the second division of the categorical syllogism: the classification into figures (ashkal). A 'figure' (shakl) is defined by the position of the middle term relative to the two extreme terms in the premises. The author enumerates three possibilities. If the middle term is the predicate in one premise and the subject in the other, that is the first figure. If it is predicate in both premises, that is the second figure. If it is subject in both premises, that is the third figure. The example given earlier (body/composed/temporally originated) is of the first figure, where 'composed' (middle) is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. The other two figures are defined analogously. This classification is foundational for evaluating the validity of syllogistic moods. The passage ends here, but typically a fourth figure is also recognized in later texts.

Key Terms
شكل
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The figure of a categorical syllogism is defined by whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise, yielding three figures.

?

The Syllogism

The Three Terms and Two Premises of a Syllogism
Ghazali defines the three terms and names the premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three terms (minor, middle, major) in any categorical syllogism and correctly name the two premises.

Core Idea
Every categorical syllogism has three terms; the middle term connects the extremes.

Before you can construct or evaluate a syllogism, you must recognize its building blocks: three terms and two premises. Ghazali explains how each term is named and how the premises get their names from the extreme terms. Mastering this basic anatomy is essential for all later logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion (natija)
The conclusion is the statement that follows from the premises. Look for a claim that is supported by the other statements. In Ghazali's example, 'Every body is created' is the conclusion.
2
Identify the subject and predicate of the conclusion
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (hadd asghar); the predicate is the major term (hadd akbar). In 'Every body is created', 'body' is the minor term and 'created' is the major term.
The major term can be more general than the minor, but it may also be equal.
3
Locate the term common to both premises
The middle term (hadd awsat) appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In Ghazali's example, 'composed' appears in both premises and connects 'body' and 'created'.
The middle term is the linking element that makes deduction possible.
4
Label the premises as major and minor
The premise that contains the major term (predicate of conclusion) is the major premise (muqaddima kubra). The premise that contains the minor term (subject of conclusion) is the minor premise (muqaddima sughra). Check each premise against the conclusion's subject and predicate.
The middle term is shared, so it cannot be used to name the premises.
5
Verify with a fiqh example
Apply the same steps to Ghazali's fiqh syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is khamr; every khamr is haram; so every intoxicant is haram'. Identify the three terms and the two premises as he does.
The fiqh example confirms that the logical structure works identically in legal reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت انقسام كل قياس إلى ثلاثة أمور مفردة فاعلم أن هذه المفردات تُسمّى حدوداً، ولكلّ واحد من الحدود الثلاثة اسم مفرد ليتميز عن غيره. أما الحدّ المشترك فَيُسمّى الحدّ الأوسط، وأما الآخران فَيُسمّى أحدهما الحدّ الأكبر والآخر الأصغر. والأصغر هو الذي يكون موضوعاً في النتيجة، والأكبر هو الذي يكون محمولاً فيها . وإنما سُمّي أكبر لأنه يمكن أن يكون أعم من الموضوع وإن أمكن أن يكون مساوياً . الحد وأما الموضوع فلا يتصوّر أن يكون أعم من المحمول، وإذا وضع كذلك كان الحكم كاذباً كقولك : كلّ حيوان إنسان، فإنه كاذب وعكسه صادق . ثم لما مست الحاجة إلى تعريف المقدمتين باسمين ولم يمكن أن يشتق اسمهما من الحدّ الأوسط لأنه مشترك فيهما اشتق اسمهما من الحدين الآخرين فَسُمِّي الذي فيه الحد الأكبر، وهو محمول النتيجة مقدمة كبرى والذي فيه موضوعها، وهو الأصغر، مقدمة صغرى ؛ فالقياس الذي أوردناه مثالاً فيه ثلاثة حدود الجسم والمؤلف والمحدث والمؤلف هو الحدّ الأوسط، والجسم هو الأصغر، والمحدث هو الحد الأكبر. وقولنا: كلّ جسم مؤلف هي المقدمة الصغرى، وقولنا : كلّ مؤلف محدث هي المقدمة الكبرى، واللازم عنه هو التقاء الحدّين الواقعين على الطرفين وهو المطلوب أولاً والنتيجة آخراً، وهو قولنا : فكلّ جسم محدث ؛ ومثاله من الفقه : كلّ مُسكر خمر وكلّ خمر حرام، فكل مسكر حرام فالمسكر والخمر والحرام حدود القياس، والخمر هو الحد الأوسط، والمسكر هو الحدّ الأصغر، والحرام هو الحد الأكبر، وقولنا : كل مسكر خمر هي الصغرى، وقولنا : كلّ خمر حرام هي المقدمة الكبرى، فهذه قسمة للقياس باعتبار أجزائه المفردة .
When you know that every syllogism is divided into three individual things, know that these individuals are called terms (hudud), and each of the three terms has a distinct name to distinguish it. The common term is called the middle term, and the other two are called the major term and the minor term. The minor term is what is the subject in the conclusion, and the major term is what is the predicate in it. It is called major because it can be more general than the subject, although it can be equal. As for the subject, it cannot be more general than the predicate; if it is so, the judgment is false, like saying 'Every animal is human,' which is false, while its converse is true. Then when there was need to name the two premises with two names, and it was not possible to derive their names from the middle term because it is shared between them, their names were derived from the other two terms: so the premise containing the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion, is called the major premise; and the premise containing the subject of the conclusion, which is the minor term, is called the minor premise. The syllogism we gave as an example has three terms: body (jism), composed (mu'allaf), and created (muhdath); composed is the middle term, body is the minor, and created is the major. Our statement 'Every body is composed' is the minor premise, and our statement 'Every composed is created' is the major premise, and the necessary consequence is the meeting of the two extreme terms, which is the desired conclusion, namely 'Every body is created.' An example from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is haram; so every intoxicant is haram' — intoxicant, khamr, and haram are the terms of the syllogism; khamr is the middle term, intoxicant is the minor term, haram is the major term; our statement 'Every intoxicant is khamr' is the minor premise, and our statement 'Every khamr is haram' is the major premise. This is the division of the syllogism according to its individual parts.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines the three terms of a syllogism and explains how the premises are named. Ghazali begins by stating that every syllogism is composed of three individual things called 'hudud' (terms). Each term has a distinct name: the common term is the middle term, and the other two are the major and minor. The minor is the subject of the conclusion, the major is the predicate. The major is so called because it can be more general than the subject, though it may be equal. The subject cannot be more general than the predicate, as in the false statement 'Every animal is human.' Then he explains that the premises are named after the extreme terms: the premise containing the major term is the major premise, and the premise containing the minor term is the minor premise. He illustrates with two examples: first, the syllogism 'Every body is composed, every composed is created, therefore every body is created,' identifying the terms. Second, from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, every khamr is haram, therefore every intoxicant is haram.' This division of the syllogism according to its individual parts is foundational for understanding the structure of categorical arguments.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): term
حد أوسط (hadd awsat): middle term
حد أكبر (hadd akbar): major term
حد أصغر (hadd asghar): minor term
مقدمة كبرى (muqaddima kubra): major premise
مقدمة صغرى (muqaddima sughra): minor premise
نتيجة (natija): conclusion

Key Takeaway
Every categorical syllogism consists of three terms (minor, middle, major) and two premises (minor premise and major premise), named after the extreme terms.

Why the First Figure Necessarily Produces a Conclusion
Ghazali explains the transitivity principle underlying the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ghazali's argument for why the first figure yields a necessary conclusion and identify the role of inclusion (dakhil) in the transmission of the judgment.

Core Idea
The first figure works because the minor is inside the middle.

Ghazali has just given an example of a first-figure syllogism. Now he explains why it necessarily yields a conclusion. The key is the principle that a judgment on the predicate is also a judgment on the subject—provided the subject is included in the predicate. This lesson unpacks that principle, showing how the middle term acts as a conduit for the judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises and the conclusion in Ghazali's example
Find the claim that 'the body is composed' (minor premise) and 'the composed is created' (major premise). The conclusion is 'the body is created'. Write them down.
This is a classic Barbara (AAA-1) syllogism.
2
Locate Ghazali's description of the transitivity principle
Read the sentences: 'the judgment on the predicate is a judgment on the subject by necessity' and 'any judgment established for the composed is necessarily established for the body'. Identify the logical relation he is highlighting.
He is formulating the dictum de omni.
3
Understand the role of inclusion (dakhil)
Note Ghazali's phrase 'the body is inside the composed'. This inclusion is what justifies transmitting the judgment from 'composed' to 'body'. Without inclusion, the transmission would not hold.
Inclusion here is class membership: every body is a composed being.
4
Recognize why the middle term is described as the 'cause of the meeting'
Ghazali calls the middle term (composed) the 'cause (sabab) of the meeting of the two extremes' because it links the minor and major terms. The judgment travels from major via middle to minor.
This is why the first figure is considered perfect: the middle term is subject of one premise and predicate of the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثاله ما أوردنا وحصول النتيجة منه بَيِّن وحاصله يرجع إلى أن الحكم على المحمول حكم علي الموضوع بالضرورة، فمهما حكم على الجسم بالمؤلّف فكل حكم يثبت للمؤلّف فقد ثبت لا محالة للجسم، فإن الجسم داخل في المؤلّف، وإذا ثبت الحكم بالحدوث على المؤلّف فقد ثبت بالضرورة على الجسم. وإنما احتيج إلى هذا من حيث إن الحكم بالحدوث على الجسم قد لا يكون بينا بنفسه، ولكن يكون الحكم به على المؤلّف بيناً بنفسه والحكم بالمؤلّف على الجسم أيضاً بيناً، فيتعدّى الحكم الذي ليس بيناً للجسم إليه بواسطة المؤلّف الذي هو بَيِّن له، فيكون الوسط سبب التقاء الطرفين وهو تعدّي الحكم إلى المحكوم عليه
Its example is what we gave, and the production of the conclusion from it is evident. Its gist is that the judgment on the predicate is a judgment on the subject by necessity. Whenever you judge that the body is composed, any judgment established for the composed is necessarily established for the body, because the body is included in the composed. And when the judgment of createdness is established for the composed, it is necessarily established for the body. This is needed because the judgment of createdness on the body may not be evident in itself, but the judgment of it on the composed is evident in itself, and the judgment of composition on the body is also evident; so the judgment that is not evident for the body is transmitted to it via the composed which is evident for it. Thus the middle term is the cause of the meeting of the two extremes, and it is the transmission of the judgment to the subject.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage explains why the first figure always yields a valid conclusion. Ghazali states the basic principle: the judgment on the predicate is also a judgment on the subject by necessity. He illustrates with the example: body is composed; composed is created. Since the body is included in 'composed' (i.e., every body is composed), any property of 'composed' necessarily applies to 'body'. The judgment 'created' on 'composed' thus passes to 'body'. The middle term 'composed' serves as the medium for transmitting the judgment from the predicate to the subject. This is why the first figure is foundational: it directly mirrors the dictum de omni et nullo (the principle that what is true of a class is true of its members).

حكم
تعدّي الحكم
وسط

Key Takeaway
The first figure's necessity rests on the inclusion of the minor term in the middle term, which transmits the judgment from major to minor via the middle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Explain how the judgment on the middle term ('intoxicant') transmits to the minor term ('nabidh').
