Fourth Mood of Third Figure: Two Conversions
Author explains the fourth mood of the third figure with example and reduction via two conversions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure syllogism of the fourth mood to the first figure using conversion and verify the validity of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The third figure's fourth mood requires two conversions: one to transform a premise, another to transform the conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth mood of the third figure. This mood involves two affirmative premises where the major is particular. To prove its validity, the author shows how to reduce it to the first figure through two conversions. Understanding this reduction helps you master the method of syllogistic conversion and see how moods in different figures are related.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and premises
Locate the given example: 'Every body is originated' (universal affirmative minor) and 'A body is moving' (particular affirmative major). The middle term 'body' is the subject of both premises.
2
Convert the major premise
The major premise 'A body is moving' is particular affirmative. Convert it to 'Some moving thing is a body' (also particular affirmative). This becomes the minor premise in the new first-figure arrangement.
Conversion preserves truth and quality; affirmative particulars convert to themselves.
3
Form the first-figure syllogism
Now place the converted major ('Some moving thing is a body') as the minor premise and the original minor ('Every body is originated') as the major premise. The middle term 'body' is now the predicate of the minor and subject of the major, which is the first figure.
In the first figure, the middle term is predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
4
Derive the intermediate conclusion
From 'Some moving thing is a body' and 'Every body is originated', in the first figure we deduce 'Some moving thing is originated' (a particular affirmative).
This is a valid Barbara-like mood (actually Darii in first figure: minor particular, major universal).
5
Apply the second conversion
Take the intermediate conclusion 'Some moving thing is originated' and convert it: 'Some originated thing is moving'. This matches the original desired conclusion exactly.
The second conversion is necessary because the first-figure conclusion had the terms in reverse order relative to the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الرابع : موجبتان والكبرى جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية، مثاله : كلّ جسم محدث وجسم ما متحرّك فيلزم محدث ما متحرّك وذلك بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى، فيرجع إلى الأول ثم عكس النتيجة ليخرج لنا عين نتيجتنا، فتقول: متحرك ما وكلّ محدث، فيلزم أن متحركاً ما محدث وتنعكس إلى عين النتيجة الأولى وهي محدث ما متحرّك، فهذا قد تبين لك أنه إنما يحقق بعكسين أحدهما عكس المقدمة والآخر عكس النتيجة .
The fourth mood: two affirmatives, and the major is particular, produces a particular affirmative. Its example: Every body is originated, and a body is moving, so it follows that some originated is moving. That is by converting the major and making it the minor, so it returns to the first figure, then converting the result to produce for us exactly our result. So you say: A moving thing and every originated, so it follows that a moving thing is originated, and it converts to exactly the first result, which is: some originated is moving. So this has become clear to you that it is only realized by two conversions: one the conversion of the premise and the other the conversion of the result.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth mood of the third figure. In this mood, both premises are affirmative, and the major premise is particular. The example given is: 'Every body is originated' (universal affirmative minor) and 'A body is moving' (particular affirmative major), which yields the conclusion 'Some originated is moving' (particular affirmative). The author explains how to reduce this mood to the first figure by converting the major premise (so that 'a body is moving' becomes 'some moving thing is a body') and then using that as the minor in the first figure. After deriving a conclusion in the first figure, a second conversion is applied to that conclusion to obtain the original result. This demonstrates that the mood requires two conversion steps: one of the major premise and one of the conclusion.

Key Terms
عكس ('aks): Conversion of a categorical proposition: swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality (for affirmative universals become particulars, negatives remain universal). [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
شكل ثالث (shakl thalith): The third figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the subject of both premises. [Note: This is standard in Aristotelian logic; no shift.]

Reducing the Third Figure to the First
Author explains the fifth mood of the third figure with example and reduction via conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting the minor premise and verify that the conclusion follows.

Core Idea
The third figure's validity is proven by reduction to the self-evident first figure.

The fifth mood of the third figure combines a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major. The conclusion is a particular negative. But how do we know this syllogism is valid? The author shows that by converting the minor premise (from 'A body is an agent' to 'An agent is a body'), we create a first-figure syllogism that is immediately obvious. This reduction technique is a powerful tool for validating arguments in any Islamic science that uses syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their types
In the fifth mood, the minor premise is a particular affirmative (e.g., 'A body is an agent'), and the major is a universal negative (e.g., 'No one is eternal'). Note the quantity (particular vs universal) and quality (affirmative vs negative).
The minor is particular affirmative, the major is universal negative.
2
Convert the minor premise
Convert the particular affirmative minor premise: from 'A body is an agent' to 'An agent is a body'. A particular affirmative proposition converts to another particular affirmative (simple conversion). This step is crucial for reduction.
Simple conversion: subject and predicate swap, quantity and quality unchanged.
3
Reconstruct the syllogism in the first figure
Place the converted minor (now 'An agent is a body') as the minor premise, and the original major ('No body is eternal') as the major. This yields a first-figure syllogism: All agents are bodies (from the converted minor? Actually careful: the converted minor is 'An agent is a body' which is particular affirmative, not universal. The first figure requires the major to be universal negative and the minor to be universal or particular? Let's check: In the standard first figure, the minor can be particular affirmative and the major universal negative, that is valid (Ferio). So the reconstructed syllogism is: (minor) An agent is a body (particular affirmative); (major) No body is eternal (universal negative); therefore, some agent is not eternal (particular negative). This is the same conclusion.
The first figure is self-evident, so no further proof is needed.
4
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion 'Not every agent is eternal' (or 'Some agent is not eternal') is the same as the original conclusion. Since the first-figure syllogism is valid, the original third-figure syllogism is also valid.
This reduction proves validity without relying on the third figure's own rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الخامس : يتألف من مقدمتين مختلفتين في الكمية والكيفية جميعاً صغراهما موجبة جزئية وكبراهما سالبة كلية ينتج جزئية سالبة، ومثاله قولك: جسم ما فاعل ولا واحد أزلي، فيلزم ليس كلّ فاعل أزلياً لأن الصغرى تنعكس إلى قولك : فاعل ما جسم فتضم إلى الكبرى القائلة ولا جسم واحد أزلي فتلزم هذه النتيجة بعينها من الشكل الأول البين بنفسه .

Its example is your saying: A body is an agent, and no one is eternal, so it follows that not every agent is eternal. Because the minor converts to your saying: An agent is a body, then you join it to the major which says: No body is eternal, so this same conclusion follows from the first figure which is self-evident.

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth mood of the third figure. The premises differ in both quantity and quality: the minor is particular affirmative, the major is universal negative. The example: 'A body is an agent' (particular affirmative) and 'No one is eternal' (universal negative). The conclusion is 'Not every agent is eternal' (particular negative). The reduction to the first figure is done by converting the minor premise from 'A body is an agent' to 'An agent is a body' (particular affirmative). This converted minor is then combined with the original major 'No body is eternal' (universal negative) in the first figure to yield the same conclusion. The author notes that the first figure is 'self-evident' (bayyin bi-nafsihi), meaning its validity is clear without further proof.

The 14 Productive Syllogistic Moods
Author summarizes that the total productive moods are 14: 4 in first, 4 in second, 6 in third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall the number of productive moods in each of the three figures and explain why the 'muhmalāt' are not counted as separate.

Core Idea
14 productive moods: 4 in first, 4 in second, 6 in third—and no more.

This lesson summarizes the complete set of valid syllogistic moods across all three figures. The author gives a clean count—14 in total—and explains that some forms (muhmalāt) are discarded because they are logically equivalent to particulars. Memorizing this enumeration is essential for mastering the foundations of categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Recite the total count
Start by memorizing the total number of productive moods: 14. Then note the distribution: 4 from the first figure, 4 from the second, and 6 from the third. Write these numbers down.
This distribution is classic in Aristotelian logic and is adopted in Islamic Mantiq.
2
Understand 'muhmalāt'
The author mentions discarding 'al-muhmalāt' (unimportant ones) because they are 'in the power of the particular'—meaning they are not genuinely distinct moods but equivalent to particular propositions. Ask yourself: why would a mood be equivalent to a particular? Reflect that particular propositions (like 'some A is B') do not yield universal conclusions in many figures, so those moods are not counted separately.
The term muhmal here is non-standard; it refers to moods that collapse into particulars.
3
Identify the three figures' moods
Recall that the first figure's productive moods are Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio (4). The second figure has Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco (4). The third figure has Darapti, Felapton, Datisi, Disamis, Ferison, Bocardo (6).
These names are mnemonic devices; you do not need to memorize them for this lesson, but they help connect to the count.
4
Explain why non-productive moods are omitted
The author says there is no benefit in detailing moods that are not productive. Think about the purpose of syllogistic: to produce certain conclusions. Non-productive moods yield no conclusion or only a weak one; hence they are excluded from the canonical list.
This is a practical judgment to focus learning on what works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذن المنتج من التأليفات أربعة عشر تأليفاً أربعة من الشكل الأول، وأربعة من الثاني وستة من الثالث وذلك بعد إسقاط المهملات فإنها في قوة الجزئية، وما عدا ذلك فليس بمنتج ولا فائدة لتفصيل ما لا إنتاج له، ومن أراد الإرتياض بتفصيله قدر عليه إذا تأمل فيه .
So then the productive among the combinations are fourteen combinations: four from the first figure, four from the second, and six from the third. That after discarding the unimportant ones (al-muhmalāt), for they are in the power of the particular. And other than that is not productive, and there is no benefit in detailing what has no production. And whoever desires exercise by detailing it is able to do so if he reflects upon it.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage gives a summary of the total number of valid syllogistic moods (ḍurūb) across the three figures. The author states that there are 14 productive combinations: 4 in the first figure, 4 in the second, and 6 in the third. He mentions that he has discarded the 'unimportant' (muhmalāt) forms, which are equivalent to particulars. The implication is that some moods that are supposedly distinct in form are logically equivalent to particular propositions and thus not counted separately. The author advises that there is no benefit in enumerating the non-productive moods, but if someone wants the exercise of detailing them, they can do so by reflection.

Counting Syllogism Combinations and Common Properties
Author answers a question about total possible combinations (48) and states common properties for all valid syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of possible premise combinations for categorical syllogisms and identify the common properties that all valid syllogisms share.

Core Idea
Only 14 out of 48 possible premise pairs yield valid syllogisms; the rest fail two basic rules.

In this passage, the author answers two important questions: how many possible ways can we combine premises in the three figures? And what common features do all valid syllogisms share? Understanding the enumeration helps you appreciate why later figures have fewer valid moods, and the common properties give you a quick filter to spot invalid arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of counting
Recognize that each premise has two qualities (affirmative/negative) and two quantities (universal/particular). So each premise can be one of four types: A, E, I, O. For two premises, there are 4 × 4 = 16 possible combinations per figure.
The author multiplies 4 by 4 to get 16 per figure.
2
Apply counting to three figures
Since the three figures differ only by the position of the middle term, each figure can have any of the 16 combinations. Thus total possible combinations across all figures = 3 × 16 = 48.
3
Identify the productive ones
Not all 16 per figure are valid. The author says only 14 combinations across all three figures are productive (valid). The rest (34) are invalid because they violate common properties.
These 14 are the known valid moods in classical logic.
4
State the common property
Every valid syllogism must have at least one affirmative premise and at least one universal premise. Therefore, no valid syllogism can have two negatives or two particulars. This rule applies to all figures.
This is a necessary condition, not sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فكم عدد الاقترانات الممكنة في هذه الأشكال؟ قلنا: ثمانية وأربعون اقتراناً في كلّ شكل ستة عشر، وذلك لأن المقدمتين المقترنتين إما كليتان أو جزئيتان أو إحداهما كلّية والأخرى جزئية، وعلى كل حال فهما إما موجبتان أو سالبتان أو واحدة موجبة والأخرى سالبة فهذه ستة عشر اقتراناً ناتجة من ضرب أربع في أربع وهي جارية في الأشكال الثلاثة، فتكون الجملة أخيراً ثمانية وأربعين والمنتج أربعة عشر اقتراناً فيبقى أربعة وثلاثون. فإن قيل: فما خواص الأشكال؟ قلنا: أما الذي يعمّ كلّ شكل فهو أنه لا بد في اقترانها من موجبة وكلية، فلا قياس عن سالبتين ولا عن جزئيتين .
If it is said: How many possible combinations are there in these figures? We say: Forty-eight combinations, in each figure sixteen. That is because the two linked premises are either both universal, both particular, or one universal and the other particular. And in each case they are either both affirmative, both negative, or one affirmative and the other negative. So these are sixteen combinations resulting from multiplying four by four, and they run through the three figures, so the total finally is forty-eight. And the productive are fourteen combinations, so thirty-four remain. If it is said: What are the properties of the figures? We say: As for that which is common to every figure, it is that in their combinations there must be an affirmative and a universal. So there is no syllogism from two negatives nor from two particulars.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage contains a question-and-answer section. First, the author calculates the total number of possible premise-pair combinations: there are 16 per figure, so 48 total. He derives 16 by considering the four possibilities for quantity (both universal, both particular, first universal-second particular, first particular-second universal) and the four possibilities for quality (both affirmative, both negative, first affirmative-second negative, first negative-second affirmative). Multiplying 4x4 gives 16. Out of these 16 per figure, only some are productive (14 total across all figures). The second question asks about properties (khawāṣṣ) of the figures. The author first gives the common property: every valid syllogism must have one affirmative premise and one universal premise. Thus, syllogisms with two negatives or two particulars are invalid. This is a general rule for all three figures.

اقتران
خاصة

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that valid syllogisms are rare: only 14 out of 48 possible premise pairs work, because each figure requires at least one affirmative and one universal premise.

Specific Properties of the Three Syllogistic Figures
Author details the specific properties of each figure regarding middle term, premises, and conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which figure a categorical syllogism belongs to based on the position of the middle term and the conditions on premises and conclusions.

Core Idea
Each figure has exclusive properties defining its valid syllogisms.

Having learned the definition of the three figures, we now study their specific properties (khawāṣṣ). The author explains for each figure the constraints on the middle term, the premises, and the conclusions. These properties are crucial for quickly assessing the validity and output of any categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three aspects of figure properties
Notice that the author examines each figure under three headings: middle term position, premises, and conclusions. This systematic approach allows precise comparison.
2
Analyze the first figure properties
First figure: middle term is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor. The minor premise must be affirmative and the major universal. It can produce all four conclusion types (A, E, I, O). Its unique property: no particular negative premise ever appears.
The first figure is the only one that can yield universal affirmative conclusions.
3
Analyze the second figure properties
Second figure: middle term is predicate in both premises. The premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). It only produces negative conclusions (never affirmative).
Second figure conclusions are always negative, either universal or particular.
4
Analyze the third figure properties
Third figure: middle term is subject in both premises. The minor premise must be affirmative. Its most specific property is that the major premise may be particular. It only yields particular conclusions (never universal).
Third figure is the only one where the major can be particular.
5
Compare the figures using the properties
Use the properties to distinguish figures. For example, if both premises are affirmative and the middle term is subject in both, it is third figure. If one premise is negative and the middle term is predicate in both, it is second figure.
Mastering these properties allows immediate figure identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما خاصية الشكل الأول فإما في وسطه وهو أن يكون محمولاً في المقدمة الأولى موضوعاً في الثانية، وإما في مقدماته وهو أن تكون الصغرى موجبة والكبرى كلّية، وإما في نتائجه وهو أن ينتج المطالب الأربعة وهي : الإيجاب الكلي، والسلب الكلّي، والإيجاب الجزئي، والسلب الجزئي. والخاصية الحقيقية التي لا يشاركه فيها شكل من الأشكال أنه لا يكون فيها (أي مقدماته) سالبة جزئية . وأما الشكل الثاني فخاصيته في وسطه أن يكون محمولاً على الطرفين وفي مقدماته ألا يتشابها في الكيفية بل تكون أبداً إحداهما سالبة والأخرى موجبة، وأما في الإنتاج فهو أنه لا ينتج موجبة أصلاً بل لا ينتج إلَّا السالب . وأما الشكل الثالث فخاصيّته في الوسط أن يكون موضوعاً للطرفين وفي المقدمات أن تكون الصغرى موجبة وأخص خواصه أنه يجوز أن تكون الكبرى منه جزئية. وأما في الإنتاج فهي أن الجزئية هي اللازمة منه دون الكلية.
As for the specific property of the first figure: either in its middle term – that it is predicate in the first premise and subject in the second – or in its premises – that the minor is affirmative and the major is universal – or in its conclusions – that it produces the four demands: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. And the real property in which no figure shares with it is that it does not have (i.e., its premises) a particular negative. As for the second figure: its property in its middle term is that it is predicate of both extremes; in its premises, that they do not resemble each other in quality, but rather one is always negative and the other affirmative; and as for production, it does not produce an affirmative at all, but only produces the negative. As for the third figure: its property in the middle term is that it is subject of both extremes; in its premises, that the minor is affirmative; and its most specific property is that its major may be particular; and as for production, it is that the particular is necessarily produced from it, not the universal.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the specific properties (khawāṣṣ) of each of the three figures. For the first figure: (1) middle term is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor; (2) premises: minor must be affirmative, major universal; (3) conclusions: it can produce all four types (A, E, I, O). Its unique property is that it never has a particular negative premise. For the second figure: (1) middle term is predicate in both premises; (2) premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); (3) conclusions: it only yields negative conclusions (never affirmative). For the third figure: (1) middle term is subject in both premises; (2) minor must be affirmative; (3) its most specific property is that the major may be particular (unlike the first two figures where the major must be universal? Actually in the first figure major must be universal, in the second it is not stated but implied? The third figure allows a particular major); (4) conclusions: it only yields particular conclusions (never universal).

The figure of a syllogism is determined by the position of the middle term, and each figure has fixed constraints on premise quality/quantity and conclusion type.

Why the First Figure is Called First
Explanation of the naming hierarchy of syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first figure is called first and how other figures are reduced to it.

Core Idea
The first figure is self-evidently productive; others gain validity by reduction to it.

In this passage, the author addresses a common question: why are the three figures of the categorical syllogism ordered as first, second, and third? The answer is not arbitrary but based on the self-evidence of the first figure and the type of conclusion each figure yields. Understanding this hierarchy helps you appreciate why logicians prioritise the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The passage begins with a question about the naming of the figures. This sets up the author's explanation.
2
Locate the answer for the first figure
The author states it is called first because its productivity (intaj) is self-evident (bayyin al-intaj). This means its validity is immediately clear without needing proof.
3
Understand reduction
Other figures are shown to be valid by reducing them to the first figure through conversion (al-'aks) or assumption (al-iftirad). This establishes the first figure as the standard of syllogistic validity.
Reduction is a key logical technique.
4
Note the implication
The first figure is foundational; all other figures depend on it for their validity. This explains why it is called 'first'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فلم سُمّي ذلك أولاً وذاك ثانياً وهذا ثالثاً؟ قلنا : سُمّي ذلك أولاً لأنه بَيِّن الإنتاج وإنما يظهر الإنتاج فيما عداه بالردّ إليه إما بالعكس أو بالافتراض، وإنما كان ذاك ثانياً وهذا ثالثاً لأن الثاني ينتج الكلي، والثالث إنما ينتج الجزئي، والكلّي أشرف من الجزئي، فكان والياً لما هو أشرف بإطلاق، وإنما كان الكلي أشرف لأن المطالب العلمية المحصلة للنفس كمالاً إنسانياً مورثاً للنجاة والسعادة إنما هي الكليات والجزئيات إن أفادت علماً فبالعرض. فإن قيل : فهل لكم في تمثيل المقاييس الأربعة عشر أمثلة فقهية، لتكون
If it is said: Why is that called first, that second, and this third? We say: That is called first because its productivity is self-evident, and productivity in others appears only by reduction to it, either by conversion or by assumption. And that one was second and this one third because the second produces the universal, and the third produces only the particular. And the universal is nobler than the particular, so it is appropriate for what is more noble absolutely. And the universal is nobler because the scientific demands that perfect the soul with human perfection, bequeathing deliverance and happiness, are universals; particulars, if they yield knowledge, then only accidentally. If it is said: Do you have fiqh examples for the fourteen syllogisms, so that they may be...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage addresses the rationale behind the naming of the three figures. The first figure is called 'first' because its validity is self-evident (bayyin al-intaj); all other figures are reduced to it through conversion or assumption to demonstrate their validity. The second figure is called 'second' because it produces universal conclusions, which are nobler, and the third figure produces only particular conclusions. The hierarchy is based on the nobility of the conclusions: universal knowledge is considered superior because it perfects the soul humanly and leads to salvation and happiness, whereas particular knowledge yields benefit only accidentally. The passage ends with a request for fiqh examples to illustrate the fourteen productive moods, indicating that the author intends to provide such examples (likely on the following page).

Key Terms
بَيِّن الإنتاج (bayyin al-intāj): self-evident productivity
الردّ (al-radd): reduction

Key Takeaway
The first figure is foundational because its validity is self-evident, and all other figures are validated by reduction to it.

Core Idea

The passage explains why the second figure is called second and the third figure third: because the second produces universal conclusions, while the third produces only particular ones. The author then argues that universals are nobler, as they perfect the human soul and bequeath deliverance and happiness. This reflects the high value placed on universal principles in Islamic thought.

Count of Possible Syllogistic Combinations
Author enumerates total possible syllogisms and specifies productive ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible syllogistic moods in any figure and identify the proportion of productive moods.

Core Idea
Only a fraction of premise pairs yield valid conclusions.

The author now answers a key practical question: how many possible combinations of premises exist in the three figures? This passage shows you the method of counting all possible moods and then tells you that only 14 out of 48 are productive. Understanding this enumeration is essential for recognizing why some syllogisms work and others do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the variables
Each premise has two binary properties: quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative).
2
Calculate per premise possibilities
There are 2 possibilities for quantity × 2 for quality = 4 types per premise.
3
Calculate per figure combinations
Since there are two premises, 4 × 4 = 16 possible mood combinations per figure.
4
Calculate total across figures
There are 3 figures, so 16 × 3 = 48 total combinations. The author states that only 14 are productive (muntij), leaving 34 non-productive.
The author does not list which moods are productive in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فكم عدد الاقترانات الممكنة في هذه الأشكال؟ قلنا: ثمانية وأربعون اقتراناً في كلّ شكل ستة عشر، وذلك لأن المقدمتين المقترنتين إما كليتان أو جزئيتان أو إحداهما كلّية والأخرى جزئية، وعلى كل حال فهما إما موجبتان أو سالبتان أو واحدة موجبة والأخرى سالبة فهذه ستة عشر اقتراناً ناتجة من ضرب أربع في أربع وهي جارية في الأشكال الثلاثة، فتكون الجملة أخيراً ثمانية وأربعين والمنتج أربعة عشر اقتراناً فيبقى أربعة وثلاثون.
If it is said: How many possible combinations are there in these figures? We say: Forty-eight combinations, in each figure sixteen. That is because the two combined premises are either both universal or both particular or one universal and the other particular, and in any case they are either both affirmative or both negative or one affirmative and the other negative. So these are sixteen combinations resulting from multiplying four by four, and they run in the three figures. So the total finally is forty-eight, and the productive ones are fourteen combinations, leaving thirty-four.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage enumerates all possible combinations of premises in the three figures of the categorical syllogism. The author calculates that there are 48 possible combinations (16 per figure) because each premise can be universal or particular (2 possibilities) and affirmative or negative (2 possibilities), giving 4 types per premise, so 4×4=16 per figure. Across three figures, that totals 48. Of these, only 14 combinations are productive (yield a valid conclusion), leaving 34 non-productive. This enumeration is foundational for understanding which moods of the syllogism are valid.

Properties of the Three Figures
Author explains the common property and specific properties of each figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the common property and the specific properties of each of the three figures of categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Every valid syllogism needs an affirmative universal; each figure has unique restrictions on terms, premises, and conclusions.

The three figures of categorical syllogism have both a shared requirement and individual characteristics that determine valid mood. This passage from al-Ghazali's Mi'yār al-'Ilm lays out these properties clearly. Understanding them is essential for constructing sound arguments and recognizing invalid ones in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the common property
Note that every valid syllogism must contain at least one affirmative premise and one universal premise. Two negatives or two particular premises never yield a conclusion.
This is a necessary condition for all figures.
2
Learn the first figure's properties
The middle term is predicate in the first premise and subject in the second. Minor premise must be affirmative, major universal. It produces all four types of conclusions. Uniquely, it never has a negative particular premise.
The first figure is the most powerful and natural.
3
Learn the second figure's properties
The middle term is predicate of both extremes. Premises differ in quality: one negative, one affirmative. It produces only negative conclusions, never affirmative.
All valid moods in the second figure are negative.
4
Learn the third figure's properties
The middle term is subject of both extremes. Minor premise is affirmative. The major may be particular. It produces only particular conclusions (affirmative or negative).
The third figure never yields a universal conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فما خواص الأشكال؟ قلنا : أما الذي يعمّ كلّ شكل فهو أنه لا بد في اقترانها من موجبة وكلية ، فلا قياس عن سالبتين ولا عن جزئيتين . وأما خاصية الشكل الأول فإما في وسطه وهو أن يكون محمولاً في المقدمة الأولى موضوعاً في الثانية، وإما في مقدماته وهو أن تكون الصغرى موجبة والكبرى كلّية، وإما في نتائجه وهو أن ينتج المطالب الأربعة وهي : الإيجاب الكلي، والسلب الكلّي، والإيجاب الجزئي، والسلب الجزئي. والخاصية الحقيقية التي لا يشاركه فيها شكل من الأشكال أنه لا يكون فيها (أي مقدماته) سالبة جزئية . وأما الشكل الثاني فخاصيته في وسطه أن يكون محمولاً على الطرفين وفي مقدماته ألا يتشابها في الكيفية بل تكون أبداً إحداهما سالبة والأخرى موجبة، وأما في الإنتاج فهو أنه لا ينتج موجبة أصلاً بل لا ينتج إلَّا السالب . وأما الشكل الثالث فخاصيّته في الوسط أن يكون موضوعاً للطرفين وفي المقدمات أن تكون الصغرى موجبة وأخص خواصه أنه يجوز أن تكون الكبرى منه جزئية. وأما في الإنتاج فهي أن الجزئية هي اللازمة منه دون الكلية.
If it is said: What are the properties of the figures? We say: As for what is common to every figure, it is that in their combinations there must be an affirmative and a universal, so there is no syllogism from two negatives nor from two particulars. As for the specific property of the first figure, it is either in its middle term: that it is a predicate in the first premise and a subject in the second, or in its premises: that the minor is affirmative and the major is universal, or in its conclusions: that it produces the four sought results, namely: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. And the real property in which no other figure shares is that there is no negative particular (premise) in it. As for the second figure, its specific property in its middle is that it is a predicate of both extremes, and in its premises is that they do not resemble each other in quality, but rather one is always negative and the other affirmative. As for production, it does not produce an affirmative at all, but only produces the negative. As for the third figure, its specific property in the middle is that it is a subject of both extremes, and in the premises is that the minor is affirmative, and the most specific of its properties is that it is permissible for its major to be particular. As for production, the particular is necessary from it, not the universal.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the properties of the three figures. The common property is that every valid syllogism must contain at least one affirmative and one universal premise; no syllogism can have two negatives or two particulars. Then each figure's specific properties are given. For the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the major and subject in the minor; the minor premise must be affirmative; the major must be universal; it can produce all four types of conclusions; uniquely, it never has a negative particular premise. The second figure has the middle term as predicate of both extremes; the premises differ in quality (one negative, one affirmative); it only produces negative conclusions. The third figure has the middle term as subject of both extremes; the minor is affirmative; the major may be particular; it only produces particular conclusions. These properties are essential for determining which moods are valid in each figure.

خاصية
مقدمة
صغرى
كبرى

B) Second figure

D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Every valid syllogism requires an affirmative and universal premise, and each figure has specific constraints on the middle term and the premises that determine which conclusions are possible.

Why the Figures Are Ordered: Priority and Nobility
Author explains the rationale for ordering the three figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for ordering the three syllogistic figures and articulate why universal knowledge is considered nobler than particular knowledge in the Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
The ordering of the figures reflects logical self-evidence and the nobility of universal knowledge.

This lesson unpacks a short but significant passage where the author justifies why the first figure is called 'first', the second 'second', and the third 'third'. The answer is not arbitrary: it rests on two principles – the self-evident validity of the first figure and the epistemological supremacy of universal conclusions. Understanding this ordering helps you appreciate how classical logicians prioritized arguments and why universal knowledge is central to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dialogical setup
The passage begins with an objection: 'Why is that called first, that second, and this third?' The author responds directly. This question-and-answer form is common in Islamic scholarly texts. Note that the author is defending a classification, not simply stating it.
The objection signals that the naming is not obvious; it requires justification.
2
Grasp why the first figure is first
The author says the first figure is 'bayyin al-intāj' (evident in production), meaning its validity is self-evident. Other figures are shown to be valid only by reducing them to the first figure via conversion (ʿaks) or supposition (iftirāḍ). Thus the first figure is the logical foundation.
This is a standard claim in Aristotelian logic: the first figure is the only perfect syllogism; all others are imperfect.
3
Distinguish the second and third figures based on conclusion type
The second figure produces universal conclusions, while the third figure produces only particular conclusions. The author declares universals 'nobler' (ashraf) than particulars, so the second figure ranks higher than the third.
The ranking is not based on validity but on the epistemological value of the conclusion.
4
Understand the epistemological reason for the nobility of universals
The author explains that universals are the true objects of scientific inquiry that perfect the human soul and lead to salvation; particulars yield knowledge only accidentally. This connects logic to the higher purposes of Islamic knowledge (maqāṣid al-sharīʿa).
This reflects the Islamic emphasis on universal principles in fiqh, kalam, and other disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فلم سُمّي ذلك أولاً وذاك ثانياً وهذا ثالثاً؟ قلنا : سُمّي ذلك أولاً لأنه بَيِّن الإنتاج وإنما يظهر الإنتاج فيما عداه بالردّ إليه إما بالعكس أو بالافتراض، وإنما كان ذاك ثانياً وهذا ثالثاً لأن الثاني ينتج الكلي، والثالث إنما ينتج الجزئي، والكلّي أشرف من الجزئي، فكان والياً لما هو أشرف بإطلاق، وإنما كان الكلي أشرف لأن المطالب العلمية المحصلة للنفس كمالاً إنسانياً مورثاً للنجاة والسعادة إنما هي الكليات والجزئيات إن أفادت علماً فبالعرض.
If it is said: Why is that called first, that second, and this third? We say: That is called first because its production is evident, and production in others becomes apparent only by reduction to it, either by conversion or by supposition. And that (the second) is called second and this third because the second produces the universal, and the third only produces the particular, and the universal is nobler than the particular, so it (the second) is a guardian over what is nobler absolutely. And the universal is nobler because the scientific demands that perfect the soul with human perfection, causing salvation and happiness, are only universals; particulars, even if they benefit knowledge, do so accidentally.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage explains the naming of the three figures. The first figure is called first because its validity is self-evident (bayyin al-intāj); all other figures must be reduced to it through conversion (ʿaks) or supposition (iftirāḍ) to demonstrate validity. The second figure is called second because it produces universal conclusions, which are nobler (ashraf) than particular ones; thus it ranks second. The third figure produces only particular conclusions and is ranked third. The author then explains why universals are nobler: they are the true objects of scientific inquiry that perfect the soul and lead to salvation; particulars yield knowledge only accidentally. This ranking reflects the epistemological priority of universal knowledge in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

الرد
كلي

Key Takeaway
The ordering of syllogistic figures is based on logical self-evidence (first) and the nobility of universal conclusions (second over third).

First Figure Syllogisms in Fiqh Examples
Author provides four fiqh examples of valid first figure syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mood and validity of each first figure syllogism from fiqh examples.

Core Idea
The first figure can produce all four categorical conclusions.

In this lesson, we examine four concrete examples of first figure syllogisms drawn from fiqh. Each example illustrates one of the four types of categorical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. By working through these examples, you will learn to recognise the structure of a valid first figure syllogism and its corresponding mood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In each example, find the term that is common to both premises.
For example 1, the middle term is 'wine' (خمر).
2
Determine the major and minor premises
The major premise contains the middle term and the predicate of the conclusion; the minor premise contains the middle term and the subject of the conclusion. Classify each premise accordingly.
In example 1: major premise is 'every wine is forbidden', minor premise is 'every intoxicant is wine'.
3
Classify each proposition as A, E, I, or O
Determine the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of each proposition. A=universal affirmative, E=universal negative, I=particular affirmative, O=particular negative.
For example 1: 'Every intoxicant is wine' is A; 'every wine is forbidden' is A; conclusion 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is A. So mood is AAA.
4
Verify the figure and validity
Check that the middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise (first figure). Confirm the mood is valid for the first figure (AAA, EAE, AII, EIO).
All four examples are valid first figure moods: AAA (Barbara), EAE (Celarent), AII (Darii), EIO (Ferio).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة الشكل الأول : هي ۱ - كلّ مسكر خمر - وكلّ خمر حرام - فكل مسكر حرام . ۲ - كلّ مسكر خمر ـ ولا خمر واحد حلال - فلا مسكر واحد حلال . ۳ - بعض الأشربة خمر ـ وكلّ خمر حرام - فبعض الأشربة حرام . ۴ - بعض الأشربة خمر - ولا خمر واحد حلال - فليس كلّ شراب حلالاً .
Examples of the first figure: 1 - Every intoxicant is wine - and every wine is forbidden - so every intoxicant is forbidden. 2 - Every intoxicant is wine - and no wine is permissible - so no intoxicant is permissible. 3 - Some beverages are wine - and every wine is forbidden - so some beverages are forbidden. 4 - Some beverages are wine - and no wine is permissible - so not every beverage is permissible.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage provides four concrete examples of valid first figure syllogisms drawn from fiqh. Each example corresponds to one of the four types of categorical conclusions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The first example: 'Every intoxicant is wine; every wine is forbidden; therefore every intoxicant is forbidden.' This is a universal affirmative conclusion (Barbara mood). The second example: 'Every intoxicant is wine; no wine is permissible; therefore no intoxicant is permissible' (Celarent mood). The third example gives a particular affirmative conclusion (Darii mood): 'Some beverages are wine; every wine is forbidden; therefore some beverages are forbidden.' The fourth gives a particular negative (Ferio): 'Some beverages are wine; no wine is permissible; therefore not every beverage is permissible' (i.e., some beverages are not permissible). These examples show the first figure can produce all four types. The subject matter ('intoxicant', 'wine', 'beverages', 'forbidden', 'permissible') is from Islamic jurisprudence, specifically rules about khamr and lawful substances.

خمر

A) Universal affirmative
B) Universal negative
C) Particular affirmative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
The first figure can produce all four types of categorical conclusions, and its valid moods are AAA, EAE, AII, and EIO.

Reducing Second Figure Syllogisms: Fiqh Examples
Author provides four fiqh examples of valid second figure syllogisms with reduction notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a valid second figure categorical syllogism to the first figure using conversion or supposition, applying the method to fiqh examples from riba rules.

Core Idea
Second figure is valid only for negative conclusions; reduction shows it depends on first figure.

This lesson presents four real syllogisms from Islamic jurisprudence on usury (riba). Each is in the second figure and the author shows how to reduce it to a first figure mood by converting a premise or using supposition (iftirāḍ). Mastering this reduction technique allows you to test the logical backbone of any juristic analogy.

Guided Path
1
Parse the first example
Identify terms: minor (cloth), major (usurious), middle (measured). Note the premises: 'Every cloth is measured' (A) and 'No usurious thing is measured' (E), conclusion 'No cloth is usurious' (E). This is second figure mood Cesare (EAE).
Second figure has middle term as predicate in both premises.
2
Reduce to first figure
Convert the major premise 'No usurious thing is measured' to 'No measured thing is usurious' (simple conversion of E). Now the syllogism reads: 'Every cloth is measured; no measured thing is usurious; therefore no cloth is usurious.' This is Celarent (first figure, EAE).
The author says it reduces to 'the second mood of the first' i.e., Celarent.
3
Check the other examples
Each of the remaining three examples uses conversion or supposition to reduce to a first figure mood: the second reduces to Celarent, the third and fourth reduce to Ferio (first figure, EIO) via conversion of the minor premise or by supposition (iftirāḍ).
Supposition (iftirāḍ) involves taking a premise as a given to force the desired form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة الشكل الثاني : ۱ - يرجع إلى الضرب الثاني من الأول : كلّ ثوب فهو مذروع ـ ولا ربوي واحد مذروع. بعكس هذه فلا ثوب واحد ربوي . ٢ - يرجع إلى الضرب الثاني من الأول أيضاً لا ربوي واحد مذروع، بعكس هذه وجعلها صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة وكلّ ثوب فهو مذروع، فلا ربوي واحد ثوب . ٣ ـ يرجع إلى الضرب الرابع من الأول : مُتموّل ما مذروع، ولا ربوي واحد مذروع، بعكس هذه، فَمُتموّل ما ليس بربوي. ٤ - يرجع إلى الضرب الرابع من الأول :أيضاً مُتموّل ما ليس بربوي (بالافتراض) وكلّ مطعوم ،ربوي، فَمُتموّل ما ليس بمطعوم.
Examples of the second figure: 1 - It reduces to the second mood of the first: Every cloth is measured - and no usurious thing is measured. By converting this, so no cloth is usurious. 2 - It reduces to the second mood of the first also: No usurious thing is measured, by converting this and making it the minor premise then converting the conclusion: and every cloth is measured, so no usurious thing is a cloth. 3 - It reduces to the fourth mood of the first: Something valuable is measured, and no usurious thing is measured, by converting this, so something valuable is not usurious. 4 - It reduces to the fourth mood of the first also: Something valuable is not usurious (by supposition) and every foodstuff is usurious, so something valuable is not a foodstuff.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage shows four valid second figure syllogisms with fiqh examples. The second figure only yields negative conclusions. Each example is accompanied by a note indicating which mood of the first figure it reduces to (by conversion or supposition). The first example: 'Every cloth is measured; no usurious thing is measured; therefore no cloth is usurious' reduces to the second mood (Celarent) of the first figure by converting the major premise. The second example similarly reduces. The third example: 'Something valuable is measured; no usurious thing is measured; therefore something valuable is not usurious' reduces to the fourth mood (Ferio) of the first figure. The fourth uses supposition (iftirāḍ) to yield the conclusion that something valuable is not a foodstuff. The examples draw on Islamic fiqh concepts: cloth (thawb), measured commodities (madhrūʿ), usurious items (ribawī), foodstuff (maṭʿūm). These are relevant to rules of ribā.

Key Terms
ربوي (ribawī): A commodity subject to the rules of ribā (usury/interest). [Note: Standard fiqh term.]
مذروع (madhrūʿ): Measured (by length or volume); a term used in discussing ribā categories. [Note: Specific to this fiqh context.]
الافتراض (al-iftirāḍ): Supposition; a method of reducing a syllogism to the first figure by assuming a premise. [Note: Standard logical term.]

Key Takeaway
Second figure syllogisms always produce a negative conclusion and can be reduced to first figure by conversion or supposition.

Examples of the First Figure of Categorical Syllogism in Fiqh
Author lists four first-figure syllogisms with Islamic legal terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four moods of the first figure categorical syllogism in Islamic legal examples and verify their validity.

Core Idea
The first figure yields certain conclusions; its four moods correspond to the four standard categorical propositions.

Al-Ghazali provides four concrete examples of first-figure syllogisms using fiqh terminology. Each example is a valid syllogism, and you will learn to recognize the pattern: the middle term connects the subject and predicate in a way that guarantees the conclusion. By understanding these examples, you see how logic supports legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In each example, find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (hadd al-awsat).
For instance, in the first example, 'khamr' is the middle term.
2
Determine the mood
Classify each premise and conclusion by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). Use the standard codes: A=universal affirmative, E=universal negative, I=particular affirmative, O=particular negative.
Example 1: A (Every intoxicant is khamr), A (Every khamr is forbidden), A (Every intoxicant is forbidden) → mood AAA.
3
Verify first figure structure
Check that the middle term is subject of the major premise (the one containing the predicate of the conclusion) and predicate of the minor premise (the one containing the subject of the conclusion).
In example 1, major premise: 'Every khamr is forbidden' (subject: khamr), minor: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' (predicate: khamr). Correct.
4
Check validity conditions
For the first figure, the major premise must be universal, and the minor premise must be affirmative. Confirm these for each example.
Example 2 (EAE): major is universal negative (universal), minor is universal affirmative (affirmative). Valid.

Reducing Second Figure Syllogisms to the First Figure
Author gives four examples of the second figure and shows how each reduces to the first figure via conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any second-figure categorical syllogism to a first-figure mood using conversion, and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The second figure is valid only when reduced to the first figure through conversion.

In the previous lesson, you learned the first figure's self-evident moods. Now we encounter the second figure, where the middle term is predicate in both premises. Its validity is not immediate; it must be reduced to the first figure. The author gives four examples from Islamic transactional law to show how conversion (aks) transforms second-figure moods into first-figure ones. Watch how a simple swap of subject and predicate unlocks certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
Look at the given syllogism: are both premises sharing the same predicate? That is the second figure. Note the quantity and quality of each premise to determine the mood.
The first example has universal affirmative minor and universal negative major.
2
Apply conversion to the necessary premise
Convert the premise that has the middle term as predicate. For example, 'No ribawi is measured' becomes 'No measured is ribawi' (simple conversion). This transforms the syllogism into first-figure form.
Conversion preserves truth for universal negatives and particular affirmatives.
3
Rearrange premises into first-figure order
After conversion, the middle term becomes the subject of the major premise. Ensure the minor premise comes first. Now you have a first-figure syllogism.
The author places the converted premise as the minor premise.
4
Identify the resulting first-figure mood
Once in first figure, determine the mood: e.g., in example 1, after conversion we get 'Every garment is measured; no measured is ribawi; therefore no garment is ribawi.' That is Celarent (first figure, second mood).
Celarent is universal negative conclusion.
5
Confirm validity
The first figure is perfect: the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises. If reduction succeeded, the original second-figure syllogism is valid. If conversion is impossible, the syllogism is invalid.
This step shows why the author calls reduction 'returns to' (يرجع إلى).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة الشكل الثاني : ۱ - يرجع إلى الضرب الثاني من الأول : كلّ ثوب فهو مذروع ـ ولا ربوي واحد مذروع. بعكس هذه فلا ثوب واحد ربوي . ٢ - يرجع إلى الضرب الثاني من الأول أيضاً لا ربوي واحد مذروع، بعكس هذه وجعلها صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة وكلّ ثوب فهو مذروع، فلا ربوي واحد ثوب . ٣ ـ يرجع إلى الضرب الرابع من الأول : مُتموّل ما مذروع، ولا ربوي واحد مذروع، بعكس هذه، فَمُتموّل ما ليس بربوي. ٤ - يرجع إلى الضرب الرابع من الأول :أيضاً مُتموّل ما ليس بربوي (بالافتراض) وكلّ مطعوم ربوي، فَمُتموّل ما ليس بمطعوم.
Examples of the second figure: 1- It returns to the second mood of the first: Every garment is measured, and no ribawi is measured. By converting this, no garment is ribawi. 2- It also returns to the second mood of the first: No ribawi is measured; by converting this and making it the minor premise, then converting the conclusion: Every garment is measured, so no ribawi is a garment. 3- It returns to the fourth mood of the first: Some property is measured, and no ribawi is measured; by converting this, some property is not ribawi. 4- It also returns to the fourth mood of the first: Some property is not ribawi (by assumption), and every food is ribawi, so some property is not food.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents four examples of the second figure of categorical syllogism. The second figure is characterized by the middle term being the predicate in both premises. The author shows how each example reduces to a mood of the first figure through conversion (aks) or assumption (fard). The first example reduces to the second mood (Celarent) of the first figure: by converting the second premise (no ribawi is measured) we get the minor premise, and the conclusion follows directly. The second example also reduces to Celarent but with an additional conversion of the conclusion. The third and fourth reduce to the fourth mood (Ferio) of the first figure. These reductions illustrate that the second figure's validity depends on conversion, unlike the first figure which is self-evident. The examples use terms from Islamic transactional law: garment (thawb), property (mutamawwal), ribawi items (those subject to riba), and food (mat‘um).

عكس
افتراض

Key Takeaway
Reduction to the first figure via conversion confirms the validity of second-figure syllogisms, making them derivative rather than primitive.

Reducing Third-Figure Syllogisms to the First Figure: Six Fiqhi Examples
Author gives six examples of the third figure and shows their reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any valid third-figure categorical syllogism to a first-figure mood using conversion and premise reordering, using examples from Islamic transaction law.

Core Idea
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion; every valid mood reduces to the first figure via conversion.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has the middle term as subject of both premises, producing only particular conclusions. Al-Ghazali provides six examples from fiqh al-mu'amalat (Islamic law of transactions) to illustrate how each can be reduced to a corresponding mood of the first figure through conversion (aks) of one premise. Mastering this reduction deepens your understanding of syllogistic validity and equips you to analyze legal reasoning that uses this figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion in each example
For each of the six examples, locate the two premises and the derived conclusion. Note that the middle term is the subject in both premises. The conclusion is always particular (some or not all).
For example, in the first example: premises = 'Every food is ribawi', 'Every food is measured'; conclusion = 'Some ribawi is measured'.
2
Apply conversion to one premise
Convert the premise that contains the predicate term of the conclusion. Simple conversion (aks) changes the subject and predicate and preserves the truth for universal negatives and particular affirmatives; for universal affirmatives, it becomes particular affirmative.
In the first example, convert 'Every food is ribawi' to 'Some ribawi is food'.
3
Reorder premises to fit the first figure
The first figure requires the major premise (containing the predicate of the conclusion) first, and the minor premise (containing the subject of the conclusion) second. Rearrange the converted premise accordingly.
After conversion, the premises become 'Every food is measured' (major) and 'Some ribawi is food' (minor).
4
Verify the resulting first-figure mood
Check the mood of the new syllogism and confirm it is valid in the first figure (e.g., Darii, Ferio, Celarent). This demonstrates the original third-figure syllogism is valid.
The first example becomes Darii (AII in the first figure), which is valid.
5
Confirm the conclusion is particular
Note that after reduction, the conclusion remains particular (I or O). This is a defining property of the third figure: it never yields a universal conclusion.
All six examples end with 'some' or 'not all'.

Examples of the third figure: 1- It returns to the third mood of the first: Every food is ribawi; by converting this, and every food is measured, so some ribawi is measured. 2- It returns to the fourth of the first: Every garment is property; by converting this, and no garment is ribawi, so not every property is ribawi. 3- It returns to the third of the first: Some food is measured; by converting this, and every food is ribawi, so some measured is ribawi. 4- It returns to the third of the first: Every food is ribawi, and some food is measured; by converting this and making it the minor premise, then converting the conclusion, so some ribawi is measured. 5- It returns to the fourth of the first: Some measured is property; by converting this, and no measured is ribawi, so not every property is ribawi. 6- It returns to the fourth of the first: Every movable is property, and some movable is not ribawi (by assumption), so not every property is ribawi.

Commentary
This passage presents six examples of the third figure of categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the subject of both premises. The author demonstrates how each reduces to a mood of the first figure, typically through conversion (aks) of one or both premises and sometimes adjusting the order. The third figure always yields a particular conclusion (some or not all). The first example reduces to the third mood (Darii) of the first figure: after converting the first premise 'every food is ribawi' to 'some ribawi is food', we have a valid syllogism. The second reduces to the fourth mood (Ferio) of the first figure. The examples continue, illustrating various combinations. The terms used are from Islamic law of transactions: food (mat‘um), ribawi items, property (mutamawwal), garments (thawb), and movable property (manqul).

Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Valid Moods
Defines conditional syllogism, gives examples, and states valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in a conditional syllogism and determine whether the argument is valid (modus ponens or modus tollens) or invalid.

Core Idea
In a conditional syllogism, only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields a necessary conclusion.

This lesson introduces the conditional conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyas al-sharti al-muttasil), a cornerstone of logical reasoning in Islamic theology and fiqh. You will learn its structure: a conditional premise and an exception premise. The key is to recognize which part of the conditional is affirmed or denied in the second premise, and to determine whether the argument is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then...' statement. Note the antecedent (the 'if' part, e.g., 'the world is originated') and consequent (the 'then' part, e.g., 'it has a creator').
The conditional premise is compound of two categorical propositions.
2
Identify the exception (istithna')
The second premise either affirms or denies one part of the conditional. Is it saying 'but it is originated' (affirming antecedent) or 'but it is not originated'?
The exception is linked by the word of exception (e.g., 'lakinna' or 'lākin').
3
Determine which part is affirmed/denied
Check whether the exception matches the antecedent, its negation, the consequent, or its negation. For example, 'but it is originated' affirms the antecedent.
There are four possibilities: affirming antecedent, denying antecedent, affirming consequent, denying consequent.
4
Check the mood for validity
If the exception affirms the antecedent (modus ponens) or denies the consequent (modus tollens), the argument is valid. Otherwise it is invalid.
The author's example: affirming antecedent yields 'he is an animal'; denying consequent yields 'he is not a human'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثاني : القياس الشرطي المتصل

والمقدمة الثانية لهذا القياس استثناء لإحدى قضيتي المقدمة الأولى إما المقدم أو التالي والاستثناء إما أن يكون لعين التالي أو لنقيضه أو لعين المقدّم أو لنقيضه، والمنتج منه اثنان وهو عين المقدّم ونقيض التالي. وأما عين التالي ونقيض المقدّم فلا ينتجان وبيانه أنا نقول : إن كان الشخص الذي ظهر عن بعد إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنه إنسان فليس يخفى أنه يلزم كونه حيواناً، وهذا استثناء عين المقدّم . ونقول : لكنه ليس بحيوان وهذا استثناء نقيض التالي فيلزم أنه ليس بإنسان ولزوم هذا أدق مدركاً، وهو أن يعرف أنه إذا لم يكن حيواناً لم يكن إنساناً، إذ لو كان إنساناً لكان حيواناً، كما شرطناه في الأول، ويدرك ذلك بأدنى تأمل. فأما
The second type: The conditional conjunctive syllogism. It is composed of two premises: one of them is compound of two propositions linked by a conditional particle, and the other is a single categorical proposition that is a repetition or contradictory of one of the two in the first premise, and is linked by the word of exception (istithna). Its example: If the world is originated, then it has a creator. But it is originated, so it has a creator. Our statement: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator' is compound of two categorical propositions linked by the conditional letter 'in' (if). And our statement 'but the world is originated' is a single categorical proposition linked by the letter of exception. And our statement 'so it has a creator' is the conclusion. This is of great benefit in intellectual and juristic matters. For we say: If this marriage is valid, then it confers lawfulness; but it is valid, so it confers lawfulness. And if the witr prayer is performed on a mount, then it is supererogatory; but it is performed on a mount, so it is supererogatory. And the second premise of this syllogism is the exception (istithna) of one of the two propositions of the first premise, either the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali). The exception is either for the same as the consequent or its contradictory, or for the same as the antecedent or its contradictory. The productively valid among them are two: affirming the antecedent (ayn al-muqaddam) and denying the consequent (naqeed al-tali). As for affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent, they do not produce. Its explanation: We say: If the person who appeared from afar is a human, then he is an animal. But he is a human, then it is not hidden that it follows that he is an animal; this is an exception of the antecedent. And we say: But he is not an animal; this is an exception of the contradictory of the consequent, so it follows that he is not a human. The implication of this is more subtle: that one knows that if he is not an animal, he is not a human, for if he were a human he would be an animal, as we assumed in the first. And that is grasped with minimal reflection. As for...
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major type of syllogism: the conditional conjunctive (qiyas sharti muttasil). It is a form of hypothetical syllogism where one premise is a conditional statement (if... then...) and the other premise either affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent, or denies the antecedent or affirms the consequent. The author defines its structure: two premises, the first is conditional (compound of two propositions), the second is categorical (the exception premise). He gives two examples: one from theology (world is originated -> has a creator) and two from fiqh (valid marriage -> lawfulness; witr prayer on mount -> supererogatory). He then explains the rules: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens) produce valid conclusions, while affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are invalid. He illustrates with an example: 'If a person is a human, then he is an animal. But he is a human, so he is an animal' (valid). 'But he is not an animal, so he is not a human' (also valid). He notes that the latter is more subtle because it requires understanding the contrapositive (if not animal, then not human). The passage emphasizes the utility of conditional syllogism in both rational and juristic reasoning.

Key Takeaway

Reducing Syllogisms to the First Figure
Author demonstrates reduction of third and fourth figure syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a categorical syllogism from the third or fourth figure to the first figure using conversion and composition.

Core Idea
Reduction proves validity by transforming any syllogism into the self-evident first figure.

The author demonstrates a powerful technique: converting third- and fourth-figure syllogisms into the first figure. Since the first figure is self-evidently valid, reduction shows that any syllogism can be validated by transforming it into this canonical form. This lesson walks you through the specific operations—conversion and composition—applied to each figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine whether the given syllogism is third figure (middle term subject in both premises) or fourth figure (middle term predicate in major, subject in minor). The examples in the passage are explicitly labeled.
2
Apply conversion where needed
For third figure, convert the minor premise (swap subject and predicate) to reposition the middle term. For fourth figure, conversion of the major premise may be necessary. Conversion must follow the rules: universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative, universal negative remains universal negative, etc.
The passage uses 'caks' (عكس) for conversion.
3
Rearrange premises into first-figure order
After conversion, place the premises so that the middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise (first figure). This may involve swapping the order of premises.
4
Derive the conclusion and convert if necessary
Draw the conclusion from the rearranged first-figure syllogism. If the original conclusion needed a different form, apply conversion to get the desired final proposition.
In the third-figure example, the conclusion 'ribawi is something measured' is obtained after converting the first-figure conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - يرجع إلى ثالث الأول : كلّ مطعوم ربوي، ومطعوم ما مكيل، بعكس هذه وجعلها صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة فربوي ما مكيل.
ه - يرجع إلى رابع الأول : مذروع ما مُتموّل بعكس هذه ولا مذروع واحد ربوي، فليس كل مُتموّل ربوياً.
- يرجع إلى رابع الأول : كلّ منقول مُتموّل ومنقول ما ليس بربوي (بالافتراض) فليس كلّ مُتموّل ربوياً.
هذا ما أردنا شرحه من أمثلة القياسات الحملية وأقسامها؛ ولنخض في الصنف الثاني.
4 – It returns to the third of the first: Every edible is ribawi, and edible is something measured; by converting these and making it the minor premise, then converting the result, so ribawi is something measured. 5 – It returns to the fourth of the first: [Every] cubit-measured is valuable; by converting this, and no single cubit-measured is ribawi, so not every valuable is ribawi. – It returns to the fourth of the first: Every transported is valuable, and transported is not ribawi (by assumption), so not every valuable is ribawi. This is what we intended to explain of examples of categorical syllogisms and their divisions; and we shall delve into the second kind.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents three examples of reducing categorical syllogisms that are not in the first figure to the first figure. The author uses the terms 'third of the first' and 'fourth of the first' to refer to the third and fourth figures, indicating that these syllogisms are reduced to the first figure. The first example: take a third-figure syllogism like 'Every edible is ribawi and every edible is measured.' To reduce it, the minor premise ('edible is measured') is converted (to 'some measured is edible') and then made the minor premise in a first-figure syllogism, yielding a conclusion that is then converted to 'some ribawi is measured.' The second and third examples reduce fourth-figure syllogisms. The second: 'Every cubit-measured is valuable, and no cubit-measured is ribawi' – through conversion and composition, it yields 'not every valuable is ribawi.' The third uses an assumption (bi-l-iftirad) to treat 'transported' as not ribawi, leading to a similar conclusion. These examples illustrate the practical technique of reduction, which was a key tool for validating syllogisms by transforming them into the self-evident first figure. The author then closes the discussion of categorical syllogisms and announces the move to conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-rujūʿ): Reduction of a syllogism from a non-first figure to the first figure through logical operations like conversion and composition. [Note: In later logic, reduction is often formalized with specific rules; here it is shown through examples.]
العكس (al-ʿaks): Conversion of a proposition, i.e., swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth value (with certain restrictions). [Note: Stable term, though logicians differ on types of conversion (simple, accidental, etc.).]

Key Takeaway
Reduction transforms non-first-figure syllogisms into the first figure, proving their validity through conversion and composition.

The Syllogism
