Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Productive Forms
Author defines conditional syllogism and explains productive exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid forms of conditional syllogism and distinguish them from the two invalid forms using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are valid; the reverse forms are fallacies.

In this lesson, we break down the conditional connected syllogism (qiyas sharti muttasil). You will learn its structure, the role of the exception (istithna'), and which combinations of premises yield a necessary conclusion. We use examples from kalam and fiqh to solidify the distinction between valid and invalid forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional major premise
Locate the 'if...then' statement in the argument. Label the proposition after 'if' as the antecedent (muqaddam) and the proposition after 'then' as the consequent (tali).
2
Identify the minor premise (istithna')
Determine which part of the conditional is affirmed or denied. The minor premise either asserts or negates the antecedent or the consequent. There are four possibilities: affirming the antecedent, denying the antecedent, affirming the consequent, or denying the consequent.
The minor premise is always a categorical proposition.
3
Apply the productive rules
A valid conclusion follows only if the minor premise affirms the antecedent (giving the consequent) or denies the consequent (giving the negation of the antecedent). Denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent are invalid and do not yield a necessary conclusion.
Memorize: afirming antecedent (modus ponens) and denying consequent (modus tollens) are valid; the other two are fallacies.
4
Consider the special case of equivalence
When the antecedent and consequent are equivalent (musawi), i.e., they mutually entail each other, then all four forms become productive. Check if the conditional is a biconditional in meaning.
Example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' – these are equivalent, so denying the antecedent also works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثاني : القياس الشرطي المتصل يتركب من مقدمتين : إحداهما مركبة من قضيتين قرن بهما صيغة شرط ، والأخرى حملية واحدة هي المذكورة في المقدمة الأولى بعينها أو نقيضها، ويقرن بها كلمة الاستثناء، مثاله : إن كان العالم حادثاً فله صانع. لكنه حادث فإذن له صانع. فقولنا: إن كان العالم حادثاً فله صانع، مركب من قضيتين حمليتين قرن بهما حرف الشرط وهو قولنا إن وقولنا : لكن العالم حادث، قضية واحدة حملية قرن بها حرف الاستثناء، وقولنا فله صانع، نتيجة، وهذا مما يكثر نفعه في العقليات والفقهيات، فإنّا نقول : إن كان هذا النكاح صحيحاً فهو مفيد للحلّ، لكنه صحيح فإذن هو مفيد للحلّ، وإن كان الوتر يُؤدَّى على الراحلة فهو نفل، لكنه يُؤدّى على الراحلة فهو إذن نفل. والمقدمة الثانية لهذا القياس استثناء لإحدى قضيتي المقدمة الأولى إما المقدم أو التالي والاستثناء إما أن يكون لعين التالي أو لنقيضه أو لعين المقدّم أو لنقيضه، والمنتج منه اثنان وهو عين المقدّم ونقيض التالي. وأما عين التالي ونقيض المقدّم فلا ينتجان وبيانه أنا نقول : إن كان الشخص الذي ظهر عن بعد إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنه إنسان فليس يخفى أنه يلزم كونه حيواناً، وهذا استثناء عين المقدّم . ونقول : لكنه ليس بحيوان وهذا استثناء نقيض التالي فيلزم أنه ليس بإنسان ولزوم هذا أدق مدركاً، وهو أن يعرف أنه إذا لم يكن حيواناً لم يكن إنساناً، إذ لو كان إنساناً لكان حيواناً، كما شرطناه في الأول، ويدرك ذلك بأدنى تأمل. فأما استثناء نقيض المقدّم، وهو أنه ليس بإنسان، فلا ينتج لا نقيض التالي وهو ليس بحيوان، إذ ربما يكون فرساً، ولا عين التالي وهو أنه حيوان فربما يكون حجراً. وكذلك نقول : إن كان هذا المصلي مُحدثاً فصلاته باطلة، لكنه مُحدث فيلزم بطلان الصلاة، لكن الصلاة ليست باطلة وهو نقيض التالي، فيلزم أنه ليس بِمُحْدِث وهو نقيض المقدّم، لكنه ليس بِمُحْدِث وهو نقيض المقدّم فلا يلزم صحة الصلاة ولا بطلانها. لكن الصلاة باطلة، وهو عين التالي فلا يلزم لا كونه محدثاً ولا كونه مُتطهراً، وإنما ينتج استثناء عين التالي ونقيض المقدم، إذا ثبت أن التالي مساو للمقدّم لا أعمّ منه ولا أخصّ، كقولنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود لكن الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس غير طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود، لكن النهار موجود فالشمس طالعة، لكن النهار غير موجود فالشمس غير طالعة.
The second kind: the conditional connected syllogism. It is composed of two premises: one of them is composed of two propositions linked by a conditional particle, and the other is a single categorical proposition which is the very one mentioned in the first premise or its contradictory, and it is linked by the word of exception (istithna'). Example: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator; but it is originated, therefore it has a creator.' So our statement 'If the world is originated then it has a creator' is composed of two categorical propositions linked by the conditional particle 'in'; and our statement 'But the world is originated' is a single categorical proposition linked by the particle of exception; and our statement 'therefore it has a creator' is the conclusion. This is of great benefit in intellectual and jurisprudential matters. For we say: 'If this marriage is valid, then it is beneficial for permissibility; but it is valid, therefore it is beneficial for permissibility.' And 'If the witr prayer is performed on a riding animal, then it is supererogatory; but it is performed on a riding animal, therefore it is supererogatory.' The second premise of this syllogism is an exception (istithna') of one of the two propositions of the first premise, either the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali). The exception is either of the very consequent (ayn al-tali), or its contradictory (naqid al-tali), or of the very antecedent (ayn al-muqaddam), or its contradictory (naqid al-muqaddam). What produces a conclusion are two: the very antecedent and the contradictory of the consequent. As for the very consequent and the contradictory of the antecedent, they do not produce a conclusion. Clarification: We say 'If the person who appeared from a distance is a human, then he is an animal; but he is a human' – it is not hidden that it necessitates that he is an animal. This is exception of the very antecedent. And we say 'But he is not an animal' – this is exception of the contradictory of the consequent, so it necessitates that he is not a human. The necessity of this is more subtle to grasp: it is to know that if he is not an animal, he is not a human, because if he were a human, he would be an animal, as we assumed in the first case. This is grasped with minimal reflection. As for exception of the contradictory of the antecedent – i.e., that he is not a human – it does not produce either the contradictory of the consequent (that he is not an animal) because he may be a horse, nor the very consequent (that he is an animal) because he may be a stone. Likewise, we say: 'If this praying person is in a state of minor impurity (muhdith), then his prayer is invalid; but he is muhdith' – so it necessitates invalidity of prayer. 'But the prayer is not invalid' – this is the contradictory of the consequent, so it necessitates that he is not muhdith, which is the contradictory of the antecedent. [But] 'he is not muhdith' – this is the contradictory of the antecedent – so it does not necessitate the validity of prayer nor its invalidity. [And] 'But the prayer is invalid' – this is the very consequent – so it does not necessitate either that he is muhdith or that he is in a state of purity (mutahhir). Exception of the very consequent produces only if it is established that the consequent is equivalent (musawi) to the antecedent, neither more general nor more specific. Like our saying: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; but the sun is shining, so daytime exists; but the sun is not shining, so daytime does not exist; but daytime exists, so the sun is shining; but daytime does not exist, so the sun is not shining.'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional connected syllogism (qiyas sharti muttasil). The author explains its structure: a major premise that is a conditional statement (two propositions linked by 'if'), and a minor premise that is a categorical proposition that either affirms or denies one of the two parts of the conditional. The minor premise is called the exception (istithna'). This syllogism is productive in two cases: affirming the antecedent (istithna' 'ayn al-muqaddam) and denying the consequent (istithna' naqid al-tali). Denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent are not productive. The author provides examples from both intellectual (world and Creator) and jurisprudential (marriage validity, witr prayer) contexts. He then elaborates with a detailed example about a distant figure: if it's human, it's an animal. Affirming the antecedent (he is human) yields he is an animal; denying the consequent (he is not an animal) yields he is not human. But denying the antecedent (he is not human) does not yield a necessary conclusion because he could be a horse or stone. Similarly, affirming the consequent (he is an animal) does not yield he is human. The author also notes that in one special case, affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent can be productive: when the antecedent and consequent are equivalent (musawi), meaning they have the same extension. The sun and daytime example illustrates this: if the sun is shining, daytime exists, and vice versa. In that case, all four forms of exception are productive. This is a crucial nuance often missed by beginners.

Key Terms
القياس الشرطي المتصل
الاستثناء
المقدم
التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conditional syllogism, which two forms of exception produce a valid conclusion?
A) Affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent
B) Denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent
C) Affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent
D) All four forms are always productive

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is valid only when the minor premise affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent, unless the antecedent and consequent are equivalent, in which case all four forms are valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Negation and Compound Conditionals
Author extends conditional syllogism to cases with negation and multiple premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate conditional syllogisms that involve negative premises or compound antecedents/consequents using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms work with negations and multiple premises – just like in proofs of God's oneness.

We have already learned the basic conditional syllogism (modus ponens and modus tollens). This passage extends it in two important ways: first, the premises themselves can be negative; second, either the antecedent or consequent may consist of multiple propositions joined together. These extensions allow us to model more complex arguments, such as the proof of God's oneness or the impossibility of the soul existing before the body.

Guided Path
1
Recognize negation in conditional premises
Look at the first example: 'If God is not one, then the world is not ordered.' The minor premise affirms 'the world is ordered', which is the contradictory of the consequent. This is a valid modus tollens: from not-Q, infer not-P. Here, not-P is 'God is one' (contradictory of 'not one').
Negation can appear in either the antecedent or consequent; the logic works the same.
2
Analyze a compound antecedent
In the soul argument, the antecedent is a conjunction of four statements: knowledge is indivisible, everything indivisible does not subsist in a divisible subject, every body is divisible, knowledge inheres in the soul. The entire conjunction implies the consequent: 'the soul is not a body.' All antecedents are true, so the consequent follows necessarily.
Treat the conjunction as a single condensed premise; the syllogism is valid if all parts are true.
3
Analyze a compound consequent
In the minor's Islam example, the antecedent is 'the acceptance of a minor's Islam is valid', and the consequent is a disjunction: 'either obligatory, permissible, or supererogatory'. The minor premise negates all disjuncts, so the antecedent is false. This is a proof by contradiction.
If a conditional has a disjunctive consequent, denying all disjuncts denies the antecedent.
4
Apply the patterns to Islamic sciences
These forms appear frequently: in kalam (e.g., proving God's oneness) and fiqh (e.g., nullifying contracts). Try to identify the form in a real argument from 'Aqa'id or Usul al-Fiqh texts.
Always check that the minor premise correctly matches the exception (istithna) required for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه يتطرق إلى مقدمات هذا القياس أيضاً السلب والإيجاب، فإنك تقول : إن كان الإله ليس بواحد فالعالم ليس بمنتظم، لكن العالم منتظم فالإله واحد. وقد يكون المقدم أقاويل كثيرة والتالي يلزم الجملة كقولك : إن كان العلم الواحد لا ينقسم وكان كلّ ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بمحل منقسم، وكان كلّ جسم منقسماً وكان العلم حالاً في النفس، فالنفس إذن ليست بجسم، لكن المقدمات ثابتة ذاتية فالتالي وهو أن النفس ليست بجسم لازم وكذلك قد يكون المقدّم واحداً والتالي قضايا كثيرة إن صح إسلام الصبي فهو إما فرض وإما مباح وإما نفل، ولا يمكن شيء من هذه الأقسام فلا يمكن الصحة. وفي العقليات نقول إن كانت النفس قبل البدن موجودة فهي إما كثيرة وإما واحدة ولا يمكن لا هذا ولا ذاك فلا يمكن أن تكون قبل البدن موجودة؛ فهذه ضروب الشرطيات المتصلة، والله أعلم.
And know that negation and affirmation also apply to the premises of this syllogism. For you say: 'If God is not one, then the world is not ordered; but the world is ordered, so God is one.' And sometimes the antecedent is many statements and the consequent follows from the whole, like your saying: 'If knowledge is one and indivisible, and everything indivisible does not subsist in a divisible subject, and every body is divisible, and knowledge inheres in the soul, then the soul is therefore not a body. But the premises are self-evident essential, so the consequent – that the soul is not a body – is necessary.' And likewise, sometimes the antecedent is one and the consequent is many statements: 'If the acceptance of a minor's Islam is valid, then it is either obligatory, or permissible, or supererogatory; but none of these divisions is possible, so validity is impossible.' And in intellectual matters we say: 'If the soul existed before the body, then it is either many or one; but neither is possible, so it cannot have existed before the body.' These are the modes of connected conditionals. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage addresses two extensions of the conditional syllogism. First, the premises themselves can be negative: for example, 'If God is not one, then the world is not ordered; but the world is ordered, so God is one.' This uses denying the consequent (the world is ordered is the contradictory of 'not ordered') to infer the contradictory of the antecedent, which is 'God is one' (the contradictory of 'not one'). Second, the antecedent or consequent may consist of multiple propositions linked together. The author gives an example where the antecedent is a conjunction of several premises (knowledge is indivisible, etc.) and the consequent follows from the entire conjunction. This is a chain of reasoning, and if all the antecedents are true, the consequent is necessary. Conversely, the antecedent may be a single proposition and the consequent a disjunction (either obligatory, permissible, or supererogatory). If the consequent is disproved (none of the options is possible), the antecedent must be false. This is a form of proof by contradiction. The author ties these to both jurisprudential (minor's Islam) and theological (soul prior to body) examples. The section ends with a closing statement.

Key Terms
المقدم أقاويل كثيرة
السلب والإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Denying the consequent
C) Affirming the consequent
D) Denying the antecedent

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms can handle negative premises and compound antecedents or consequents, greatly expanding their power in theological and legal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the argument 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore it has a cause.' Identify the form and check validity. Then rewrite it using negation: 'If the world is not temporally originated, then it does not have a cause. The world has a cause. Therefore it is temporally originated.' Is this valid? Explain.
?
In usul al-fiqh, consider: 'If a sale is valid, then either the price is known or the delivery is immediate. The price is not known and delivery is not immediate. Therefore the sale is not valid.' Identify the logical form and the type of consequent (disjunctive). Determine validity.

The Disjunctive Syllogism: Forms and Application in Islamic Jurisprudence
Author defines disjunctive syllogism, gives examples, and explains the four valid exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid forms of a disjunctive syllogism and apply them to examples from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The disjunctive syllogism is the logical engine behind the juristic method of sabr wa taqsim.

In this passage, the author introduces the third kind of conditional syllogism: the disjunctive syllogism, which jurists and theologians know as sabr wa taqsim. By understanding its four productive forms, you gain a tool for eliminating possibilities and arriving at certain conclusions in legal reasoning. The example of the world being either eternal or originated illustrates how the minor premise (exception) leads to a definitive conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
Locate the major premise that presents two mutually exclusive possibilities. In the passage, it is 'The world is either eternal or originated'.
2
Determine the type of disjunction
The passage assumes an exclusive 'or' (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) where exactly one disjunct is true. This is crucial for the four forms to be valid.
Inclusive 'or' only permits two of the four forms.
3
Recognize the minor premise as an exception
The minor premise (e.g., 'but it is originated') affirms or negates exactly one of the disjuncts. This is called istithnā'.
4
Derive the conclusion
Based on which disjunct is affirmed/negated, the conclusion is the negation or affirmation of the other disjunct. For example, if 'it is originated' is affirmed, the conclusion is 'it is not eternal'.
5
List the four productive forms
From the passage, note all four combinations: affirm A → negate B; affirm B → negate A; negate A → affirm B; negate B → affirm A. Each is valid because the disjunction excludes the middle.
These correspond to modus ponendo tollens and modus tollendo ponens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثالث : القياس الشرطي المنفصل وهو الذي يسميه الفقهاء والمتكلمون السبر والتقسيم ومثاله قولنا : العالم إما قديم وإما محدث، لكنه محدث فهو إذن ليس بقديم. وقولنا: إما قديم وإما محدث مقدمة واحدة وقولنا لكنه محدث مقدمة أخرى هي استثناء إحدى قضيتي المقدمة الأولى بعينها، فأنتج نقيض الآخر وينتج فيه أربعة استثناءات، فإنك تقول منه : إما قديم وإما محدث، لكنه قديم فهو ليس بمحدث، لكنه ليس بقديم فهو محدث، لكنه ليس بمحدث فهو قديم.
The third kind: the disjunctive conditional syllogism. It is what the jurists and theologians call 'sabr and taqsim' (division and elimination). Its example is our statement: 'The world is either eternal or originated; but it is originated, therefore it is not eternal.' And our statement: 'Either eternal or originated' is one premise, and our statement 'But it is originated' is another premise which is an exception of exactly one of the two propositions of the first premise, so it produces the contradictory of the other. Four exceptions are productive in it. For you say: 'Either eternal or originated; but it is eternal, so it is not originated; but it is not eternal, so it is originated; but it is not originated, so it is eternal.'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage introduces the disjunctive syllogism (qiyas sharti munfasil), identified with the jurists' 'sabr and taqsim' (trial and division). The example 'The world is either eternal or originated' is a complete disjunction (exclusive 'or'). The minor premise affirms one disjunct (e.g., 'it is originated'), and the conclusion negates the other. The author notes that four forms of exception are productive: (1) affirming one disjunct leads to negating the other; (2) negating one leads to affirming the other; (3) and (4) similarly from the other side. This is because in a true exclusive disjunction, exactly one disjunct is true. The four productive forms correspond to: affirming A → negating B; affirming B → negating A; negating A → affirming B; negating B → affirming A. This is the basis of the 'sabr wa taqsim' method used in fiqh and kalam to narrow down possibilities for determining the 'illa (cause) of a ruling.

Key Terms
القياس الشرطي المنفصل
السبر والتقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is not originated

D) It may be either

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive syllogism, when its major premise is an exclusive disjunction, yields four valid forms based on affirming or negating one disjunct to conclude about the other.

Valid and Invalid Exception Forms in Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains rules of exception with fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four forms of exception (istithnā') in a conditional syllogism are valid and which are fallacious, using Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are always valid; denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent are fallacies.

In conditional syllogisms (qiyās shartī muttaṣil), the exception premise (istithnā') picks out one part of the conditional. Not all forms of exception yield a valid conclusion. The author of the Mīyār al-'Ilm illustrates this with a fiqh example: 'If this praying person is in hadath, his prayer is invalid.' Let's learn which inferences follow and which do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional proposition
Look for an 'if...then...' statement in the Arabic. In the passage, there is: 'If this praying person is in hadath, then his prayer is invalid.'
The conditional is the major premise.
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent
The antecedent (muqaddam) is 'he is in hadath'; the consequent (tālī) is 'his prayer is invalid'.
3
Identify the exception premise
The exception premise affirms or negates either the antecedent or the consequent. There are four possibilities: affirming antecedent, negating antecedent, affirming consequent, negating consequent.
4
Apply the rules of validity
Affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens) are always valid. Denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent are fallacious unless the antecedent and consequent are logically equivalent.
The passage shows that denying antecedent (he is not in hadath) does not prove the prayer is valid or invalid; affirming consequent (prayer invalid) does not prove he is in hadath.
5
Verify with the passage's reasoning
The author explicitly states that negating the antecedent does not yield the negation of the consequent, nor does affirming the consequent yield the antecedent, using the examples of horse and stone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه ليس استثناء نقيض المقدّم، وهو أنه ليس بإنسان، فلا ينتج لا نقيض التالي وهو بحيوان، إذ ربما يكون فرساً، ولا عين التالي وهو أنه حيوان فربما يكون حجراً. وكذلك نقول : إن كان هذا المصلي مُحدثاً(۱) فصلاته باطلة، لكنه مُحدث فيلزم بطلان الصلاة، لكن الصلاة ليست باطلة وهو نقيض التالي، فيلزم أنه ليس بِمُحْدِث وهو نقيض المقدّم، لكنه ليس بِمُحْدِث وهو نقيض المقدّم فلا يلزم صحة الصلاة ولا بطلانها. لكن الصلاة باطلة، وهو عين التالي فلا يلزم لا كونه محدثاً ولا كونه مُتطهراً، وإنما ينتج استثناء عين التالي ونقيض المقدم، إذا ثبت أن التالي مساو للمقدّم لا أعمّ منه ولا أخصّ، كقولنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود لكن الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس غير طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود، لكن النهار موجود فالشمس طالعة، لكن النهار غير موجود فالشمس غير طالعة. واعلم أنه يتطرق إلى مقدمات هذا القياس أيضاً السلب والإيجاب، فإنك تقول : إن كان الإله ليس بواحد فالعالم ليس بمنتظم، لكن العالم منتظم فالإله واحد. وقد يكون المقدم أقاويل كثيرة والتالي يلزم الجملة كقولك : إن كان العلم الواحد لا ينقسم وكان كلّ ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بمحل منقسم، وكان كلّ جسم منقسماً وكان العلم حالاً في النفس، فالنفس إذن ليست بجسم، لكن المقدمات ثابتة ذاتية فالتالي وهو أن النفس ليست بجسم لازم وكذلك قد يكون المقدّم واحداً والتالي قضايا كثيرة إن صح إسلام الصبي فهو إما فرض وإما مباح وإما نفل، ولا يمكن شيء من هذه الأقسام فلا يمكن الصحة. وفي العقليات نقول إن كانت النفس قبل البدن موجودة فهي إما كثيرة وإما واحدة ولا يمكن لا هذا ولا ذاك فلا يمكن أن تكون قبل البدن موجودة؛ فهذه ضروب الشرطيات المتصلة، والله أعلم. (۱) المُحْدِثُ : الذي خرج منه ريح أو بول أو غائط .
That it is not an exception of the negation of the antecedent—that is, not being human—so it does not produce the negation of the consequent, which is being an animal, for perhaps it is a horse, nor does it produce the affirmation of the consequent, which is being an animal, for perhaps it is a stone. And similarly we say: If this praying person is in a state of ritual impurity (hadath), then his prayer is invalid; but he is in a state of ritual impurity, so the invalidity of prayer follows; but the prayer is not invalid, and that is the negation of the consequent, so it follows that he is not in a state of ritual impurity, which is the negation of the antecedent. But he is not in a state of ritual impurity—the negation of the antecedent—so it does not follow that the prayer is valid or invalid. But the prayer is invalid, and that is the affirmation of the consequent, so it does not follow that he is in a state of ritual impurity nor that he is in a state of ritual purity. Rather, the exception of the affirmation of the consequent and the negation of the antecedent yields a result only when it is established that the consequent is equivalent to the antecedent, neither more general nor more specific, as in our saying: If the sun is rising, then the daytime exists; but the sun is rising, so the daytime exists; but the sun is not rising, so the daytime does not exist; but the daytime exists, so the sun is rising; but the daytime does not exist, so the sun is not rising. And know that negation and affirmation also apply to the premises of this syllogism, for you say: If the deity is not one, then the world is not orderly; but the world is orderly, so the deity is one. And sometimes the antecedent consists of many statements and the consequent follows from the whole, as in your saying: If knowledge is indivisible, and everything indivisible does not subsist in a divisible subject, and every body is divisible, and knowledge inheres in the soul, then the soul is therefore not a body; but the premises are established as essential, so the consequent—that the soul is not a body—follows. And similarly, sometimes the antecedent is one and the consequent consists of many propositions: If the Islam of a boy is valid, then it is either obligatory, permissible, or supererogatory; but none of these divisions is possible, so validity is not possible. And in rational matters we say: If the soul existed before the body, then it is either many or one; but neither this nor that is possible, so it cannot be that it existed before the body. These are the modes of conditional syllogisms, and God knows best. (1) al-muḥdith: one from whom has come out wind, urine, or excrement.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules of exception (istithnā') in conditional syllogisms (qiyās shartī muttaṣil). The author explains what happens when the exception is not properly matched to the logical relationship between antecedent and consequent. He begins by showing that if you take the negation of the antecedent (e.g., 'not human'), it does not entail the negation of the consequent (e.g., 'not animal') because the consequent might be broader—the thing could be a horse. Similarly, taking the affirmation of the consequent (e.g., 'animal') does not entail the antecedent because it could be a stone. He then illustrates with a fiqh example: 'If this praying person is in a state of hadath, his prayer is invalid.' If we affirm the antecedent (he is in hadath), we get the consequent (prayer invalid). If we deny the consequent (prayer not invalid), we get the negation of the antecedent (he is not in hadath). However, if we deny the antecedent (he is not in hadath), we cannot infer anything about the validity of prayer—it might be invalid for another reason. Similarly, affirming the consequent (prayer invalid) does not tell us about the antecedent. Only when the consequent is exactly equivalent to the antecedent (mutasāwī) do all four forms of exception yield valid conclusions, as in the rising sun and daytime example. The author also notes that the premises themselves can be negative or affirmative. He gives examples of compound antecedents (multiple premises leading to one consequent) and compound consequents (one antecedent leading to multiple alternatives). This passage is a detailed parsing of the logical possibilities and their conditions for validity.

Key Terms
استثناء
مقدم
تال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider the conditional: 'If a person is in a state of janābah, then his prayer is invalid.' Which of the following inferences is always valid?
A) He is in janābah, so his prayer is invalid.
B) He is not in janābah, so his prayer is valid.
C) His prayer is invalid, so he is in janābah.
D) His prayer is valid, so he is not in janābah.

Key Takeaway
In a conditional syllogism, affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are always valid; denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent are fallacies unless antecedent and consequent are logically equivalent.

(b) His prayer is invalid, so he is in janābah. (c) He is not in janābah, so his prayer is valid. (d) His prayer is valid, so he is not in janābah.

When All Four Exception Forms are Valid: The Condition of Logical Equivalence
Author explains condition of equivalence and compound conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when the antecedent and consequent in a conditional are logically equivalent (musāwī) and verify that all four forms of exception become valid, and analyze compound conditionals with multiple antecedents or consequents.

Core Idea
Only when antecedent and consequent are equivalent do denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent become valid.

The author goes beyond the basic fallacies to explain a special condition: when the antecedent and consequent are logically equivalent (musāwī), all four forms of exception yield valid conclusions. He illustrates with the classic example: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' This lesson explores that condition and also covers compound conditionals where antecedents or consequents are multiple.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional and test for equivalency
Check if the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent and the consequent necessarily implies the antecedent. In the sun/day example, both directions hold: if sun rises, daytime; if daytime, sun rises (under normal circumstances).
This is mutual implication.
2
Apply all four exception forms
With equivalence, affirming antecedent yields consequent; denying antecedent yields denying consequent; affirming consequent yields antecedent; denying consequent yields denying antecedent.
All are valid.
3
Examine compound antecedents
Sometimes the antecedent is multiple propositions joined by 'and'. The author gives the example about the soul not being a body: several premises lead to one consequent.
The exception can work on the whole conjunction.
4
Examine compound consequents
Sometimes the consequent is a disjunction of options. The author gives the example: 'If the Islam of a boy is valid, then it is either obligatory, permissible, or supererogatory.'
Denying all disjuncts negates the antecedent.
5
Note the inclusion of negation in premises
The author notes that the premises themselves may be negative or affirmative, as in the example: 'If the deity is not one, then the world is not orderly.'

أنه ليس استثناء نقيض المقدّم، وهو أنه ليس بإنسان، فلا ينتج لا نقيض التالي وهو بحيوان، إذ ربما يكون فرساً، ولا عين التالي وهو أنه حيوان فربما يكون حجراً. وكذلك نقول : إن كان هذا المصلي مُحدثاً(۱) فصلاته باطلة، لكنه مُحدث فيلزم بطلان الصلاة، لكن الصلاة ليست باطلة وهو نقيض التالي، فيلزم أنه ليس بِمُحْدِث وهو نقيض المقدّم، لكنه ليس بِمُحْدِث وهو نقيض المقدّم فلا يلزم صحة الصلاة ولا بطلانها. لكن الصلاة باطلة، وهو عين التالي فلا يلزم لا كونه محدثاً ولا كونه مُتطهراً، وإنما ينتج استثناء عين التالي ونقيض المقدم، إذا ثبت أن التالي مساو للمقدّم لا أعمّ منه ولا أخصّ، كقولنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود لكن الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس غير طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود، لكن النهار موجود فالشمس طالعة، لكن النهار غير موجود فالشمس غير طالعة. واعلم أنه يتطرق إلى مقدمات هذا القياس أيضاً السلب والإيجاب، فإنك تقول : إن كان الإله ليس بواحد فالعالم ليس بمنتظم، لكن العالم منتظم فالإله واحد. وقد يكون المقدم أقاويل كثيرة والتالي يلزم الجملة كقولك : إن كان العلم الواحد لا ينقسم وكان كلّ ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بمحل منقسم، وكان كلّ جسم منقسماً وكان العلم حالاً في النفس، فالنفس إذن ليست بجسم، لكن المقدمات ثابتة ذاتية فالتالي وهو أن النفس ليست بجسم لازم وكذلك قد يكون المقدّم واحداً والتالي قضايا كثيرة إن صح إسلام الصبي فهو إما فرض وإما مباح وإما نفل، ولا يمكن شيء من هذه الأقسام فلا يمكن الصحة. وفي العقليات نقول إن كانت النفس قبل البدن موجودة فهي إما كثيرة وإما واحدة ولا يمكن لا هذا ولا ذاك فلا يمكن أن تكون قبل البدن موجودة؛ فهذه ضروب الشرطيات المتصلة، والله أعلم. (۱) المُحْدِثُ : الذي خرج منه ريح أو بول أو غائط .
That it is not an exception of the negation of the antecedent—that is, not being human—so it does not produce the negation of the consequent, which is being an animal, for perhaps it is a horse, nor does it produce the affirmation of the consequent, which is being an animal, for perhaps it is a stone. And similarly we say: If this praying person is in a state of ritual impurity (hadath), then his prayer is invalid; but he is in a state of ritual impurity, so the invalidity of prayer follows; but the prayer is not invalid, and that is the negation of the consequent, so it follows that he is not in a state of ritual impurity, which is the negation of the antecedent. But he is not in a state of ritual impurity—the negation of the antecedent—so it does not follow that the prayer is valid or invalid. But the prayer is invalid, and that is the affirmation of the consequent, so it does not follow that he is in a state of ritual impurity nor that he is in a state of ritual purity. Rather, the exception of the affirmation of the consequent and the negation of the antecedent yields a result only when it is established that the consequent is equivalent to the antecedent, neither more general nor more specific, as in our saying: If the sun is rising, then the daytime exists; but the sun is rising, so the daytime exists; but the sun is not rising, so the daytime does not exist; but the daytime exists, so the sun is rising; but the daytime does not exist, so the sun is not rising. And know that negation and affirmation also apply to the premises of this syllogism, for you say: If the deity is not one, then the world is not orderly; but the world is orderly, so the deity is one. And sometimes the antecedent consists of many statements and the consequent follows from the whole, as in your saying: If knowledge is indivisible, and everything indivisible does not subsist in a divisible subject, and every body is divisible, and knowledge inheres in the soul, then the soul is therefore not a body; but the premises are established as essential, so the consequent—that the soul is not a body—follows. And similarly, sometimes the antecedent is one and the consequent consists of many propositions: If the Islam of a boy is valid, then it is either obligatory, permissible, or supererogatory; but none of these divisions is possible, so validity is not possible. And in rational matters we say: If the soul existed before the body, then it is either many or one; but neither this nor that is possible, so it cannot be that it existed before the body. These are the modes of conditional syllogisms, and God knows best. (1) al-muḥdith: one from whom has come out wind, urine, or excrement.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules of exception (istithnā') in conditional syllogisms (qiyās shartī muttaṣil). The author explains what happens when the exception is not properly matched to the logical relationship between antecedent and consequent. He begins by showing that if you take the negation of the antecedent (e.g., 'not human'), it does not entail the negation of the consequent (e.g., 'not animal') because the consequent might be broader—the thing could be a horse. Similarly, taking the affirmation of the consequent (e.g., 'animal') does not entail the antecedent because it could be a stone. He then illustrates with a fiqh example: 'If this praying person is in a state of hadath, his prayer is invalid.' If we affirm the antecedent (he is in hadath), we get the consequent (prayer invalid). If we deny the consequent (prayer not invalid), we get the negation of the antecedent (he is not in hadath). However, if we deny the antecedent (he is not in hadath), we cannot infer anything about the validity of prayer—it might be invalid for another reason. Similarly, affirming the consequent (prayer invalid) does not tell us about the antecedent. Only when the consequent is exactly equivalent to the antecedent (mutasāwī) do all four forms of exception yield valid conclusions, as in the rising sun and daytime example. The author also notes that the premises themselves can be negative or affirmative. He gives examples of compound antecedents (multiple premises leading to one consequent) and compound consequents (one antecedent leading to multiple alternatives). This passage is a detailed parsing of the logical possibilities and their conditions for validity.

مقدم

مساو

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
The Four Modes of the Disjunctive Syllogism
Author defines the disjunctive syllogism and presents its four exception modes for two-part opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate each of the four exception modes of a two-part disjunctive syllogism using examples from kalām and fiqh.

Core Idea
A two-part disjunction yields four valid inferences: affirm one to negate the other, or negate one to affirm the other.

The disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyās sharṭī munfaṣil) is a cornerstone of Islamic theological and legal reasoning. Also called 'sabr wa taqsim' by jurists, it allows us to reason by elimination. In this lesson, we will examine its simplest form: a disjunction with exactly two mutually exclusive and exhaustive parts. We will learn the four possible ways to 'except' one part and what conclusions follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
In the example 'The world is either eternal (qadīm) or temporally originated (muḥdath)', recognize that this is a two-part disjunction. Note that the parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
The Arabic term for this is التَّعاندُ التَّام (complete opposition).
2
Understand the exception premise
The second premise excepts (istithnā') one part: either affirming it or negating it. This is the 'istithnā' clause.
3
Derive the conclusion
If you affirm one part, you negate the other; if you negate one, you affirm the other. This works only because the parts are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
4
List the four valid modes
1. Affirm A → not B. 2. Affirm B → not A. 3. Negate A → B. 4. Negate B → A. These are all valid.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Use a fiqh example: 'The act is either wājib or ḥarām; it is wājib, so it is not ḥarām.' This uses the first mode.
Recognize that this reasoning appears in kalām proofs for the temporality of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثالث : القياس الشرطي المنفصل وهو الذي يسميه الفقهاء والمتكلمون السبر والتقسيم ومثاله قولنا : العالم إما قديم وإما محدث، لكنه محدث فهو إذن ليس بقديم. وقولنا: إما قديم وإما محدث مقدمة واحدة وقولنا لكنه محدث مقدمة أخرى هي استثناء إحدى قضيتي المقدمة الأولى بعينها، فأنتج نقيض الآخر وينتج فيه أربعة استثناءات، فإنك تقول: لكن العالم محدث فيلزم عنه أنه ليس بقديم أو تقول : لكنه قديم فيلزم أنه ليس بمحدث، أو تقول : لكنه ليس بقديم فيلزم أنه محدث وهو استثناء النقيض، أو تقول : لكنه ليس بمحدث فيلزم منه أنه قديم؛ فاستثناء عين إحداهما ينتج نقيض الآخر، واستثناء نقيض إحداهما ينتج عين الآخر. وهذا فيما لو اقتصرت أجزاء التعاند على اثنين، فإن كانت ثلاثاً أو أكثر ولكنها تامة العناد، فاستثناء عين واحدة ينتج نقيض الآخرين، كقولك: لكنه مساو فيلزم أنه ليس أقل ولا أكثر، واستثناء نقيض واحدة لا ينتج إلا انحصار الحق في الجزأين الآخرين، كقولك: لكنه ليس مساوياً، فيلزم أن يكون إما أقل أو أكثر، فإن استثنيت نقيض الاثنين تعين الثالث، فأما إذا لم تكن الأقسام تامة العناد كقولك : هذا إما أبيض وإما أسود، أو زيد إما بالحجاز أو بالعراق، فاستثناء عين الواحد ينتج نقيض الآخر، كقولك: لكنه بالحجاز أو لكنه أسود فينتج نقيض سائر الأقسام ؛ فأما استثناء نقيض الواحد فلا لا ينتج عين الآخر ولا نقيضه، فإنه لا حاصر في الأقسام، فقولنا: ليس بالحجاز لا يوجب أن يكون في العراق، ولا ألا يكون به إلا إذا بان بطلان سائر الأقسام بدليل آخر، فعند ذلك يصير الباقي ظاهر الحصر تام العناد ولا يحتاج هذا إلى مثال في الفقه فإن أكثر نظر الفقهاء على السبر والتقسيم يدور ولكن لا يشترط في الفقهيات الحصر القطعي بل الظني فيه كالقطعي في غيره . (۲) السَّبْرُ والتقسيم (في اصطلاح الفقهاء وغيرهم): حصر الأوصاف في الأصل المقيس عليه، وإلغاء بعضها، ليتعين الباقي لِلْعِليَّة.
The third type: the disjunctive conditional syllogism. It is what jurists and theologians call 'al-sabr wa al-taqsīm' (testing and division). Its example is our saying: The world is either eternal or temporally originated; but it is temporally originated, so it is therefore not eternal. And our saying 'either eternal or temporally originated' is one premise, and our saying 'but it is temporally originated' is another premise which is the exception of one of the two propositions of the first premise itself, so it produces the negation of the other. Four exceptions are possible in it: you say 'but the world is temporally originated' so it follows that it is not eternal; or you say 'but it is eternal' so it follows that it is not temporally originated; or you say 'but it is not eternal' so it follows that it is temporally originated—that is the exception of the negation; or you say 'but it is not temporally originated' so it follows that it is eternal. So the exception of the affirmation of one of them produces the negation of the other, and the exception of the negation of one of them produces the affirmation of the other. This applies when the parts of opposition are limited to two. If they are three or more but have complete opposition, then the exception of the affirmation of one produces the negation of the others, as in your saying: 'but it is equal' so it follows that it is neither less nor more; and the exception of the negation of one produces only the restriction of the truth to the other two parts, as in your saying: 'but it is not equal' so it follows that it is either less or more. If you except the negation of two, the third is determined. But if the divisions are not in complete opposition, as in your saying: 'this is either white or black' or 'Zayd is either in the Hijaz or in Iraq', then the exception of the affirmation of one produces the negation of the other, as in your saying: 'but he is in the Hijaz' or 'but it is black' so it produces the negation of the remaining divisions. As for the exception of the negation of one, it does not produce the affirmation of the other nor its negation, because there is no exhaustive division. Our saying 'he is not in the Hijaz' does not necessitate that he is in Iraq, nor that he is not there, unless the invalidity of the other divisions becomes clear by another evidence. Then the remaining becomes apparently exhaustive and in complete opposition. This does not need a fiqh example, for most of the reasoning of the jurists revolves around sabr wa taqsim, but in jurisprudential matters absolute exhaustive division is not required; rather, the conjectural is like the certain in other matters. (2) al-sabr wa al-taqsīm (in the terminology of jurists and others): restricting the attributes in the original analogized-from, and canceling some of them, so that the remaining is determined as the cause.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyās shartī munfaṣil), which jurists and theologians call al-sabr wa al-taqsīm (testing and division). The author begins with the classic kalām example: 'The world is either eternal or temporally originated; but it is temporally originated, so it is not eternal.' He explains that the disjunctive premise is a single premise with two parts, and the second premise is the exception (istithnā') of one of those parts. There are four possible exceptions: affirming one part to negate the other, affirming the other to negate the first, negating one to affirm the other, and negating the other to affirm the first. When there are exactly two mutually exclusive and exhaustive parts, these four inferences are valid. Then the author addresses cases with three or more parts that are 'complete opposition' (tāmmat al-‘inād), meaning exhaustive and mutually exclusive. In such cases, affirming one part negates all others, and negating one part narrows the truth to the remaining parts. He gives the example of a quantity being equal, less, or more. If parts are not completely opposed (e.g., colors like white and black, which are not exhaustive), then affirming one does negate the other, but negating one does not affirm the other because other possibilities exist. The author notes that in fiqh, jurists frequently use sabr wa taqsim, but they do not require absolute certainty of exhaustiveness; conjectural exhaustiveness suffices in legal reasoning. The footnote provides the technical definition of sabr wa taqsim as used by jurists.

Key Terms
القياس الشرطي المنفصل
السبر والتقسيم
التعاند
تامة العناد

C) It is either B or not B.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
In a two-part disjunction with complete opposition, the four exception modes produce valid conclusions: affirming one to negate the other or negating one to affirm the other.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Complete and Incomplete Opposition in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author extends the disjunctive syllogism to cases with three or more parts and distinguishes complete from incomplete opposition, with a note on fiqh application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete opposition in a disjunctive syllogism and determine when a conclusion is valid or merely probable based on the exhaustiveness of the disjuncts.

Core Idea
Not all disjunctions are exhaustive; the validity of an inference depends on whether the parts are truly in complete opposition.

The disjunctive syllogism becomes more complex when there are more than two parts. The author introduces the concept of 'complete opposition' (tāmmat al-‘inād), where the parts are exhaustive and mutually exclusive. In such cases, the inference patterns are still valid, but with nuances. When the opposition is incomplete (like 'white or black'), some inferences fail. This lesson explores these distinctions, which are crucial for proper use of sabr wa taqsim in fiqh, where absolute exhaustiveness is not always required—conjectural exhaustiveness suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the number of parts in the disjunction
In the example 'The quantity is equal, less, or more', there are three parts. Recognize that this is a multi-part disjunction.
2
Determine if the opposition is complete
Are the parts mutually exclusive and exhaustive? For 'equal, less, more', yes—a quantity can only be one of these, and no other possibility exists. This is complete opposition (tāmmat al-‘inād).
3
Apply the rules for complete opposition with multiple parts
If you affirm one part, you can negate all others. If you negate one part, you narrow the truth to the remaining parts (but do not definitely pick one).
The author gives the example: 'but it is equal → it is not less and not more'; and 'but it is not equal → it is either less or more'.
4
Contrast with incomplete opposition
Example: 'This is either white or black'. These are mutually exclusive but not exhaustive (it could be red). Affirming one negates the other, but negating one does not affirm the other because a third option is possible.
The author says: استثناء نقيض الواحد لا ينتج عين الآخر (excepting the negation of one does not produce the affirmation of the other).
5
Understand the fiqh context: conjectural exhaustiveness
In usul al-fiqh, sabr wa taqsim is used to identify the 'illa (effective cause). Complete enumeration is often not possible, but a conjecture (ẓann) of exhaustiveness is accepted as sufficient in legal reasoning. The author states: 'the conjectural is like the certain in other matters'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثالث : القياس الشرطي المنفصل وهو الذي يسميه الفقهاء والمتكلمون السبر والتقسيم ومثاله قولنا : العالم إما قديم وإما محدث، لكنه محدث فهو إذن ليس بقديم. وقولنا: إما قديم وإما محدث مقدمة واحدة وقولنا لكنه محدث مقدمة أخرى هي استثناء إحدى قضيتي المقدمة الأولى بعينها، فأنتج نقيض الآخر وينتج فيه أربعة استثناءات، فإنك تقول: لكن العالم محدث فيلزم عنه أنه ليس بقديم أو تقول : لكنه قديم فيلزم أنه ليس بمحدث، أو تقول : لكنه ليس بقديم فيلزم أنه محدث وهو استثناء النقيض، أو تقول : لكنه ليس بمحدث فيلزم منه أنه قديم؛ فاستثناء عين إحداهما ينتج نقيض الآخر، واستثناء نقيض إحداهما ينتج عين الآخر. وهذا فيما لو اقتصرت أجزاء التعاند على اثنين، فإن كانت ثلاثاً أو أكثر ولكنها تامة العناد، فاستثناء عين واحدة ينتج نقيض الآخرين، كقولك: لكنه مساو فيلزم أنه ليس أقل ولا أكثر، واستثناء نقيض واحدة لا ينتج إلا انحصار الحق في الجزأين الآخرين، كقولك: لكنه ليس مساوياً، فيلزم أن يكون إما أقل أو أكثر، فإن استثنيت نقيض الاثنين تعين الثالث، فأما إذا لم تكن الأقسام تامة العناد كقولك : هذا إما أبيض وإما أسود، أو زيد إما بالحجاز أو بالعراق، فاستثناء عين الواحد ينتج نقيض الآخر، كقولك: لكنه بالحجاز أو لكنه أسود فينتج نقيض سائر الأقسام ؛ فأما استثناء نقيض الواحد فلا لا ينتج عين الآخر ولا نقيضه، فإنه لا حاصر في الأقسام، فقولنا: ليس بالحجاز لا يوجب أن يكون في العراق، ولا ألا يكون به إلا إذا بان بطلان سائر الأقسام بدليل آخر، فعند ذلك يصير الباقي ظاهر الحصر تام العناد ولا يحتاج هذا إلى مثال في الفقه فإن أكثر نظر الفقهاء على السبر والتقسيم يدور ولكن لا يشترط في الفقهيات الحصر القطعي بل الظني فيه كالقطعي في غيره . (۲) السَّبْرُ والتقسيم (في اصطلاح الفقهاء وغيرهم): حصر الأوصاف في الأصل المقيس عليه، وإلغاء بعضها، ليتعين الباقي لِلْعِليَّة.
The third type: the disjunctive conditional syllogism. It is what jurists and theologians call 'al-sabr wa al-taqsīm' (testing and division). Its example is our saying: The world is either eternal or temporally originated; but it is temporally originated, so it is therefore not eternal. And our saying 'either eternal or temporally originated' is one premise, and our saying 'but it is temporally originated' is another premise which is the exception of one of the two propositions of the first premise itself, so it produces the negation of the other. Four exceptions are possible in it: you say 'but the world is temporally originated' so it follows that it is not eternal; or you say 'but it is eternal' so it follows that it is not temporally originated; or you say 'but it is not eternal' so it follows that it is temporally originated—that is the exception of the negation; or you say 'but it is not temporally originated' so it follows that it is eternal. So the exception of the affirmation of one of them produces the negation of the other, and the exception of the negation of one of them produces the affirmation of the other. This applies when the parts of opposition are limited to two. If they are three or more but have complete opposition, then the exception of the affirmation of one produces the negation of the others, as in your saying: 'but it is equal' so it follows that it is neither less nor more; and the exception of the negation of one produces only the restriction of the truth to the other two parts, as in your saying: 'but it is not equal' so it follows that it is either less or more. If you except the negation of two, the third is determined. But if the divisions are not in complete opposition, as in your saying: 'this is either white or black' or 'Zayd is either in the Hijaz or in Iraq', then the exception of the affirmation of one produces the negation of the other, as in your saying: 'but he is in the Hijaz' or 'but it is black' so it produces the negation of the remaining divisions. As for the exception of the negation of one, it does not produce the affirmation of the other nor its negation, because there is no exhaustive division. Our saying 'he is not in the Hijaz' does not necessitate that he is in Iraq, nor that he is not there, unless the invalidity of the other divisions becomes clear by another evidence. Then the remaining becomes apparently exhaustive and in complete opposition. This does not need a fiqh example, for most of the reasoning of the jurists revolves around sabr wa taqsim, but in jurisprudential matters absolute exhaustive division is not required; rather, the conjectural is like the certain in other matters. (2) al-sabr wa al-taqsīm (in the terminology of jurists and others): restricting the attributes in the original analogized-from, and canceling some of them, so that the remaining is determined as the cause.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyās shartī munfaṣil), which jurists and theologians call al-sabr wa al-taqsīm (testing and division). The author begins with the classic kalām example: 'The world is either eternal or temporally originated; but it is temporally originated, so it is not eternal.' He explains that the disjunctive premise is a single premise with two parts, and the second premise is the exception (istithnā') of one of those parts. There are four possible exceptions: affirming one part to negate the other, affirming the other to negate the first, negating one to affirm the other, and negating the other to affirm the first. When there are exactly two mutually exclusive and exhaustive parts, these four inferences are valid. Then the author addresses cases with three or more parts that are 'complete opposition' (tāmmat al-‘inād), meaning exhaustive and mutually exclusive. In such cases, affirming one part negates all others, and negating one part narrows the truth to the remaining parts. He gives the example of a quantity being equal, less, or more. If parts are not completely opposed (e.g., colors like white and black, which are not exhaustive), then affirming one does negate the other, but negating one does not affirm the other because other possibilities exist. The author notes that in fiqh, jurists frequently use sabr wa taqsim, but they do not require absolute certainty of exhaustiveness; conjectural exhaustiveness suffices in legal reasoning. The footnote provides the technical definition of sabr wa taqsim as used by jurists.

Key Terms
التعاند
تامة العناد
السبر والتقسيم
الحصر القطعي
الظني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a disjunctive syllogism with three parts in complete opposition (e.g., 'The quantity is either equal, less, or more'), if you except the negation of one part (e.g., 'It is not equal'), what is the conclusion?
A) It is less.
B) It is more.
C) It is either less or more.
D) It is neither less nor more.

Key Takeaway
In complete opposition, negating one part narrows the possibilities to the remaining parts; in incomplete opposition, negating one part does not affirm any other part because of unenumerated alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disjunction: 'The Arabic word is either a verb (fi‘l), noun (ism), or particle (ḥarf).' If you know it is not a noun, what can you conclude? Is this opposition complete? Explain.
?
In a fiqh context, the possible causes (attributes) for the prohibition of an item are: intoxicating, harmful, or impure. If you eliminate harm and impurity, what remains? Is this a valid conclusion in complete opposition? How does the jurist's requirement for exhaustive enumeration differ from strict logical requirements?

The Syllogism
beginner

Reductio ad Absurdum (Qiyas al-Khalf): Structure and Example
Author defines reductio ad absurdum and gives a fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a reductio ad absurdum argument and trace the logical flow in a fiqh example.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum uses a false conclusion to expose a false premise.

The author introduces the fourth type of syllogism: the reductio. Unlike a direct syllogism where both premises are true, here one premise is taken as apparently true and the other is doubtful; the resulting false conclusion shows the doubtful premise is false. Let's walk through the fiqh example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The major premise is 'Every obligatory (farḍ) prayer is not performed on a mount.' The minor premise is 'Witr is obligatory (farḍ).'
The major premise is assumed to be true (apparently true) for the purpose of argument.
2
Derive the conclusion
From the two premises, the conclusion follows: 'Witr is not performed on a mount.' This is a valid categorical syllogism (first figure, mood EAE).
The logical form is sound; the issue is with a premise's truth.
3
Recognize the conclusion is false
We know from fiqh that the witr prayer can be performed on a mount in certain circumstances, so the conclusion is false.
The falsity of the conclusion is the key indicator.
4
Locate the false premise
Since the major premise is taken as true, the falsity must reside in the minor premise. Therefore, 'Witr is obligatory (farḍ)' is false; witr is a confirmed sunnah, not an obligatory farḍ.
The reductio forces us to reject the minor premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الرابع : في قياس الخلف وصورته صورة القياس الحملي، ولكن إذا كانت المقدمتان صادقتين سُمّي قياساً مستقيماً، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين ظاهرة الصدق والأخرى كاذبة أو مشكوك فيها وأنتج نتيجة بينة الكذب ليستدلّ بها على أن المقدمة كاذبة، سُمّي قياس خلف. ومثال ذلك قولنا في الفقه (۱) : كلّ ما هو فرض فلا يُؤدَّى على الراحلة والوتر فرض فإذن لا يؤدى على الراحلة وهذه النتيجة كاذبة ولا تصدر إِلَّا من قياس في مقدماته مقدمة كاذبة ولكن قولنا كلّ واجب فلا يؤدى على الراحلة مقدمة ظاهرة الصدق فبقي أن الكذب في قولنا أن الوتر فرض فيكون (۱) نَصَّ الأئمة والفقهاء على أَنْ مَنْ كان راكباً على دابةٍ ولا يمكنه النزول عنها لخوف على نفسه أو ماله أو لخوف من ضرر يلحقه بالانقطاع عن القافلة أو كان بحيث لو نزل عنها لا يمكنه العودة إلى ركوبها ونحو ذلك، فإنه يصلي الفرض في هذه الأحوال على ظهر الدابة، إلى أي جهة يمكنه الاتجاه إليها وتسقط عنه أركان الصلاة التي لا يستطيع فعلها، ولا إعادة عليه. أما عند الأمن والقدرة فإنها لا تصح إلا إذا أتى بها كاملة مستوفية لشروطها وأركانها، كالصلاة على الأرض .
The fourth type: On the reductio syllogism (qiyās al-khalf). Its form is the form of the categorical syllogism, but if both premises are true, it is called a direct syllogism; if one of the premises is apparently true and the other is false or doubtful, and it produces a clearly false conclusion in order to infer from that that the premise is false, it is called a reductio syllogism. An example of that in fiqh (1): Everything that is obligatory (farḍ) is not performed on a mount; the witr prayer is obligatory; therefore, it is not performed on a mount. This conclusion is false, and it only arises from a syllogism whose premises contain a false premise. But our statement 'every obligation is not performed on a mount' is an apparently true premise, so it remains that the falsity is in our statement 'the witr prayer is obligatory', so it is (not obligatory). (1) The imams and jurists have stated that if a person is riding an animal and cannot dismount due to fear for himself, his property, or fear of harm from falling behind the caravan, or if dismounting would prevent him from remounting, and the like, then he performs the obligatory prayer in such circumstances on the back of the animal, facing any direction he can; the pillars of prayer that he cannot perform are dropped, and there is no repetition upon him. As for when there is safety and ability, the prayer is not valid unless performed completely fulfilling its conditions and pillars, like prayer on the ground.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage introduces qiyās al-khalf, or reductio ad absurdum. The author explains that it takes the form of a categorical syllogism (ḥamlī), but unlike a direct syllogism where both premises are true and yield a true conclusion, here one premise is known to be true and the other is doubtful or false. The syllogism produces a clearly false conclusion, which indicates that the doubtful premise must be false. The author provides a fiqh example: 'Every obligatory (farḍ) prayer is not performed on a mount; the witr prayer is obligatory; therefore, the witr prayer is not performed on a mount.' But it is known that in certain circumstances, obligatory prayers can be performed on a mount, and the witr is a confirmed sunnah, not an obligatory farḍ. So the conclusion is false. Since the major premise 'every obligation is not performed on a mount' is generally true under normal conditions (though there are exceptions), the falsity must lie in the minor premise that 'witr is obligatory'. Hence, witr is not truly obligatory (farḍ), but rather a sunnah. The footnote clarifies the fiqh ruling: when there is a valid excuse, even obligatory prayers can be performed on a mount, so the major premise as stated is not universally true, but in this reductio it is taken as apparent truth to expose the falsehood in the minor premise. This lesson demonstrates how reductio works in practice: assuming the falsity of a conclusion forces a reevaluation of the premises.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف (qiyās al-khalf): A syllogism that uses an apparently true premise and a doubtful premise to produce a false conclusion, thereby proving the doubtful premise false. [Note: Also called 'burhān al-khalf' in some texts; the concept is stable across the tradition.]
القياس المستقيم (al-qiyās al-mustaqīm): A syllogism with both premises true, yielding a true conclusion. [Note: No significant shift.]
ظاهر الصدق (ẓāhir al-ṣidq): A premise that appears to be true, whether it is actually true or accepted as true for the purpose of argument. [Note: This term appears in dialectical contexts; in demonstration, truth must be certain.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio example given, what does the false conclusion indicate?
A) The major premise is false.
B) The minor premise 'witr is obligatory' is false.
C) Both premises are false.
D) The argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum reveals a false premise by deriving an impossible conclusion from it together with a true premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio ad absurdum argument on a fiqh issue: Suppose someone claims that 'Every transaction involving deferred payment is riba (usury).' Show that this claim leads to a false conclusion (e.g., that a valid sale with deferred payment is riba), thereby disproving the claim.
?

Rules of Disjunctive Syllogisms with Full Contradiction
Author explains rules of disjunctive syllogisms when parts are two or more with full contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a disjunctive syllogism by applying the rules of full contradiction (ta'am al-'inad) for two or more parts.

Core Idea
In a fully contradictory disjunction, affirming one part negates all others; negating one leaves the remaining options.

Disjunctive syllogisms are a powerful tool in Islamic logic, especially in fiqh methodology where jurists use 'sabr wa taqsim' (examination and division). The key to using them correctly is understanding when the disjuncts are fully contradictory. This lesson walks you through the rules that govern these syllogisms, from the simple two-part case to more complex multipart disjunctions, and clarifies common pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction and its parts
Locate the disjunctive statement (e.g., 'the world is either originated or eternal'). List all the parts (disjuncts). Check how many there are: two, three, or more.
The number of parts affects the rule.
2
Determine if the disjunction is fully contradictory
Check if the parts are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive. For example, 'either originated or eternal' is fully contradictory because no other possibility exists. 'Either white or black' is not fully contradictory because something could be red.
Full contradiction is called 'taam al-'inad'.
3
Apply the appropriate rule based on the exception
If full contradiction holds: for two parts, affirming one yields negation of the other, and negating one yields affirmation of the other. For three or more, affirming one negates the rest, negating one leaves the remaining set, and negating two affirms the third. If not fully contradictory, affirming one still negates the others, but negating one does not yield a definite conclusion.
The rule for negating in non-full contradiction requires additional evidence to achieve exclusivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن العالم مُحدث فيلزم عنه أنه ليس بقديم أو تقول : لكنه قديم فيلزم أنه ليس بمُحدث، أو تقول : لكنه ليس بقديم فيلزم أنه مُحدث وهو استثناء النقيض، أو تقول : لكنه ليس بمُحدث فيلزم منه أنه قديم؛ فاستثناء عين إحداهما ينتج نقيض الآخر، واستثناء نقيض إحداهما ينتج عين الآخر. وهذا فيما لو اقتصرت أجزاء التعاند على اثنين، فإن كانت ثلاثاً أو أكثر ولكنها تامة العناد، فاستثناء عين واحدة ينتج نقيض الآخرين، كقولك: لكنه مساو فيلزم أنه ليس أقل ولا أكثر، واستثناء نقيض واحدة لا ينتج إلا انحصار الحق في الجزأين الآخرين، كقولك: لكنه ليس مساوياً، فيلزم أن يكون إما أقل أو أكثر، فإن استثنيت نقيض الاثنين تعين الثالث، فأما إذا لم تكن الأقسام تامة العناد كقولك : هذا إما أبيض وإما أسود، أو زيد إما بالحجاز أو بالعراق، فاستثناء عين الواحد ينتج نقيض الآخر، كقولك: لكنه بالحجاز أو لكنه أسود فينتج نقيض سائر الأقسام ؛ فأما استثناء نقيض الواحد فلا لا ينتج عين الآخر ولا نقيضه، فإنه لا حاصر في الأقسام، فقولنا: ليس بالحجاز لا يوجب أن يكون في العراق، ولا ألا يكون به إلا إذا بان بطلان سائر الأقسام بدليل آخر، فعند ذلك يصير الباقي ظاهر الحصر تام العناد ولا يحتاج هذا إلى مثال في الفقه فإن أكثر نظر الفقهاء على السبر والتقسيم يدور ولكن لا يشترط في الفقهيات الحصر القطعي بل الظني فيه كالقطعي في غيره .
But [you may say]: the world is originated, so it follows that it is not eternal; or you say: but it is eternal, so it follows that it is not originated; or you say: but it is not eternal, so it follows that it is originated—this is the exception of the contradictory; or you say: but it is not originated, so it follows that it is eternal. So excepting one of the two [directly] produces the contradictory of the other, and excepting the contradictory of one produces the other itself. This is when the parts of the contradiction are limited to two. If they are three or more but with full contradiction, then excepting one directly produces the contradictory of the others, as when you say: but it is equal, so it follows that it is neither less nor more; and excepting the contradictory of one only yields the restriction of the truth to the two other parts, as when you say: but it is not equal, so it follows that it is either less or more. If you except the contradictory of the two, the third is determined. As for when the divisions are not fully contradictory, like your saying: this is either white or black, or Zayd is either in Hijaz or in Iraq, then excepting one directly produces the contradictory of the other, as your saying: but he is in Hijaz or but he is black, so it produces the contradictory of the remaining divisions. But excepting the contradictory of one does not produce the other itself nor its contradictory, because there is no exclusive enumeration of the divisions. So our saying: he is not in Hijaz does not necessitate that he is in Iraq, nor that he is not in Iraq, unless the falsity of the remaining divisions is proven by another evidence. At that point, the remaining becomes apparently exclusive and fully contradictory. This does not need an example from fiqh, for most of the jurists' reasoning revolves around examination and division, but in jurisprudential matters, certain exclusive enumeration is not required; rather, probabilistic [enumeration] in them is like certain in other matters.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the rules of disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i munfasil) when the disjuncts are fully contradictory (ta'am al-'inad). The author first explains the two-part case: if you have a disjunction like 'either the world is originated or it is eternal', then affirming one directly (istithna' 'ayn) yields the negation of the other, and negating one (istithna' naqid) yields the affirmation of the other. Then he extends to three or more fully contradictory parts: affirming one directly negates all others; negating one does not affirm a specific other but only reduces the possibilities to the remaining set; negating two affirms the third. For parts that are not fully contradictory (like 'either white or black' when something could be red), affirming one still negates the other, but negating one does not affirm the other because other possibilities exist. This is crucial for fiqh methodology, where jurists often use examination and division (sabr wa taqsim) without requiring absolute exhaustive enumeration; probabilistic determination suffices in legal reasoning.

Key Terms
التعاند
استثناء
تام العناد

A) It is not haram but could be mubah.
B) It is neither haram nor mubah.
C) It is mubah.
D) Nothing follows.

Key Takeaway
The rules of disjunctive syllogisms depend on whether the disjuncts are fully contradictory; in fiqh, probabilistic exhaustive enumeration suffices for practical reasoning.

The Syllogism

Qiyas al-Khalf: Reductio ad Absurdum in Islamic Logic
Author defines qiyas al-khalf and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a qiyas al-khalf argument to disprove a false claim in fiqh or kalam.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum uses a false conclusion to expose a false premise.

This lesson introduces qiyas al-khalf, a logical tool used by Islamic jurists and theologians to refute false claims. The author explains how to combine an opponent's thesis with a known truth to derive an absurd conclusion, thereby proving the thesis false. You will learn the method and apply it to real debates in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Take the claim you wish to disprove and treat it as a premise in a syllogism. For example, the opponent says 'Witr is obligatory'.
This premise is the one you suspect is false.
2
Find an established true premise
Select a proposition that is known to be true and can be combined with the target claim. In the fiqh example, 'Every obligatory act is not performed on a riding beast' is taken as apparently true.
This premise must be beyond dispute or at least widely accepted.
3
Construct the categorical syllogism
Combine the two premises in a valid categorical form. Example: 'Every obligatory act is not performed on a riding beast; witr is obligatory; therefore, witr is not performed on a riding beast.'
Ensure the syllogism follows a valid mood and figure (e.g., first figure).
4
Check the conclusion
If the conclusion is clearly false (batin), then at least one premise must be false. Since the true premise is well-established, the target claim must be false. In the example, the conclusion is false because witr can be performed on a riding beast, so the opponent's claim that witr is obligatory is false.
The false conclusion is the 'absurdity' that reveals the false premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الرابع : في قياس الخلف وصورته صورة القياس الحملي، ولكن إذا كانت المقدمتان صادقتين سُمّي قياساً مستقيماً، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين ظاهرة الصدق والأخرى كاذبة أو مشكوكاً فيها وأنتج نتيجة بينة الكذب ليستدلّ بها على أن المقدمة كاذبة، سُمّي قياس خلف. ومثال ذلك قولنا في الفقه : كلّ ما هو فرض فلا يُؤدَّى على الراحلة والوتر فرض فإذن لا يؤدى على الراحلة وهذه النتيجة كاذبة ولا تصدر إِلَّا من قياس في مقدماته مقدمة كاذبة ولكن قولنا كلّ واجب فلا يؤدى على الراحلة مقدمة ظاهرة الصدق فبقي أن الكذب في قولنا أن الوتر فرض فيكون نقيضه، وهو أنه ليس بفرض، صادقاً وهو المطلوب من العقليات :قولنا كلّ ما هو أزلي فلا يكون مؤلفاً، والعالم أزلي فإذن لا يكون مؤلّفاً، لكن النتيجة ظاهرة الكذب ففي المقدمات كاذبة. وقولنا : الأزلي ليس بمؤلف ظاهر الصدق فينحصر الكذب في قولنا : العالم أزلي، فإذن نقيضه وهو أن العالم ليس بأزلي صدق وهو المطلوب؛ فطريق هذا القياس أن تأخذ مذهب الخصم وتجعله مقدمة وتضيف إليها مقدمة أخرى ظاهرة الصدق، فينتج من القياس نتيجة ظاهرة الكذب فتبيّن أن ذلك لوجود كاذبة في المقدمات. ويجوز يُسمّى هذا قياس الخلف، لأنك ترجع من النتيجة إلى الخلف، فتأخذ مطلوبك من المقدمة التي خلفتها كأنها مُسلّمة، ويجوز أن يُسمّى قياس الخلف لأن الخلف هو الكذب المناقض للصدق، وقد أدرجت في المقدمات كاذبة في معرض الصدق، ولا مشاحة في التسمية بعد فهم المعنى
The fourth type: On reductio ad absurdum. Its form is the form of categorical syllogism, but if both premises are true it is called a direct syllogism. If one premise is apparently true and the other is false or doubtful, and it produces a clearly false conclusion to indicate that the premise is false, it is called reductio ad absurdum. An example of that in fiqh: 'Every obligatory act is not performed on a riding beast, and witr is obligatory, therefore witr is not performed on a riding beast.' This conclusion is false, and it only arises from a syllogism whose premises contain a false premise. But our statement 'Every obligatory act is not performed on a riding beast' is apparently true, so the falsehood remains in our statement that 'witr is obligatory', and its contradictory, that it is not obligatory, is true—that is what is sought. An analogous example from rational matters: 'Everything eternal is not composed, and the world is eternal, therefore the world is not composed.' But the conclusion is clearly false, so there is a false premise. Our statement 'The eternal is not composed' is apparently true, so the falsehood is restricted to our statement 'the world is eternal', and its contradictory, that the world is not eternal, is true—that is what is sought. The method of this syllogism is that you take the opponent's doctrine and make it a premise, then add to it another premise that is apparently true, and the syllogism produces a clearly false conclusion, so it becomes clear that this is because there is a false premise. This may be called reductio ad absurdum because you return from the conclusion to the falsehood, taking what you seek from the premise that you left behind as if it were conceded. It may also be called reductio ad absurdum because 'falsehood' (khalf) is the contradictory of truth, and you have inserted a false premise in the guise of truth—there is no quibbling over naming after understanding the meaning.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum), a type of categorical syllogism used to disprove an opponent's claim. The author distinguishes it from a direct syllogism: in a direct syllogism, both premises are true and the conclusion is true; in reductio, one premise is the opponent's claim (taken as a premise) and the other is an established truth, and the conclusion is known to be false. This reveals that the opponent's claim must be false. Two examples are given: a fiqh example about witr prayer not being performed on a riding beast (where the false premise is that witr is obligatory, since it is actually sunnah), and a rational example about the eternity of the world (where the false premise is that the world is eternal). The method is to combine the opponent's thesis with a known true premise to deduce a false conclusion, thereby showing the thesis false. The author notes the etymology: 'khalf' means falsehood, and the proof proceeds by exposing that falsehood.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف
قياس مستقيم

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf proves a claim false by deriving an absurd consequence from it combined with a known truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
